	<item>
		<title>How Amazon Bedrock catches AI-generated phishing</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/how-amazon-bedrock-catches-ai-generated-phishing/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Radha Panchap]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Thu, 02 Jul 2026 17:55:41 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Amazon Bedrock]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Best Practices]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Foundational (100)]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">395d0b963ef226d44d24b8de1b8d6428970e860f</guid>

					<description>Social engineering through phishing remains one of the most common tactics for launching cyberattacks. AI-generated phishing email messages now pose a new challenge for security teams managing email systems, significantly raising the risk because of their advanced sophistication. Modern social engineers use generative AI and open source intelligence (OSINT) to craft thousands of unique messages […]</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;Social engineering through phishing remains one of the most common tactics for launching cyberattacks. AI-generated phishing email messages now pose a new challenge for security teams managing email systems, significantly raising the risk because of their advanced sophistication. Modern social engineers use generative AI and open source intelligence (OSINT) to craft thousands of unique messages with perfect grammar, appropriate context, and personalized details. Today, an indicator of a phishing email message might be a perfectly written, professionally formatted message.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="the-evolution-of-phishing"&gt;The evolution of phishing&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For someone like John, an IT security engineer at a mid-sized firm, the rules of phishing detection were once straightforward: flag the typos, catch the generic salutations, and quarantine anything with a mismatched sender domain. These were the defining characteristics of an earlier era of phishing, when attacks sent millions of generic, error-riddled email messages at scale, relying on volume rather than precision to find victims. Security filters were built exactly for these threats, and for years, they were effective. Poor grammar, generic greetings, and mismatched logos were indicators that gave attackers away.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The threat landscape John monitors today looks nothing like the ones those filters were designed to catch. Generative AI changed how phishing works. Attacks are now grammatically correct, contextually accurate, and personalized to the target. These messages don’t trigger traditional filters because those filters weren’t designed to catch them.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The threat is no longer identifiable by what it looks like, but what it knows. Modern AI systems run OSINT operations that pull data from professional networks, corporate websites, and publicly available digital footprints to map out organizational hierarchies and relationships. With that intelligence, social engineers can process massive datasets at scale to generate contextually accurate messages personalized to your organization. These communications can even adapt in real time based on your responses, shifting tone or adjusting details to stay consistent with the conversation.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon Bedrock is a fully managed service that makes high-performing foundation models (FMs) from leading AI companies available through a unified API, along with capabilities needed to build generative AI applications with security, privacy, and responsible AI. Amazon Bedrock adds an additional layer of analysis to your existing security infrastructure that goes beyond traditional surface-level filtering. It understands context and detects phishing attempts based on behavioral patterns, not grammar quality or formatting. To put that into practice, let’s break down how Amazon Bedrock analyzes an email from the moment it hits your inbox.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon Bedrock uses large-scale general-purpose AI models pre-trained on vast amounts of data. Foundation models can analyze behavioral patterns in email content, understand contextual relationships, and identify anomalies that signal a message might be a phishing attempt. In practice, these capabilities can be structured as a multi-stage analysis pipeline. Each email passes through authentication, behavior analysis, and risk scoring before reaching your users’ inboxes.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon Bedrock offers two integrated capabilities to power your AI-driven phishing defense. Pre-trained foundation models bring sophisticated natural language understanding that can detect nuanced manipulation, contextual anomalies, and impersonation patterns invisible to rule-based systems. The second capability, Amazon Bedrock Guardrails, provides configurable safeguards that help align foundation model interactions with your organization’s responsible AI policies and application requirements, without requiring custom detection logic. Together, these capabilities can be integrated into a multi-stage email analysis pipeline.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="amazon-bedrock-workflow-for-intelligent-phishing-defense"&gt;Amazon Bedrock workflow for intelligent phishing defense&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In the workflow solution, each message first undergoes standard authentication checks (Sender Policy Framework (SPF), DomainKeys Identified Mail (DKIM), Domain-based Message Authentication, Reporting and Conformance (DMARC)). These protocols confirm that the sending server is authorized to send on behalf of the domain and that the message hasn’t been tampered with in transit. The phishing detection workflow, powered by the Amazon Bedrock foundation models, analyzes the message against three key factors: word choice, communication style deviations, and contextual appropriateness of requests. Detecting these subtle inconsistencies in writing style and misaligned requests adds a deeper layer of analysis on top of traditional security controls. AI analysis also requires careful governance to confirm it operates responsibly and within your defined boundaries. Amazon Bedrock Guardrails help filter both input prompts and model outputs. They prevent responses that could inadvertently leak confidential data, and they check that analysis results adhere to the policies you set. Keep in mind that guardrails need careful configuration and calibration to meet your application requirements.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="implementing-amazon-bedrock-guardrails-for-analysis"&gt;Implementing Amazon Bedrock Guardrails for analysis&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon Bedrock Guardrails give you granular control over how foundation models process email content through content filters, denied topics, word filters, and sensitive information filters. For example, John the security engineer can configure guardrails to automatically redact sensitive personally identifiable information (PII) discovered during email analysis, helping to prevent the foundation model from generating responses that could inadvertently leak confidential data.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;However, guardrail configurations for security analysis require careful calibration. While content filters protect against inappropriate inputs and outputs, overly restrictive settings can prevent the model from analyzing suspicious content that legitimately needs to be evaluated. If a social engineer includes offensive language in an email message to bypass filters, your guardrails must allow the security system to analyze that content. At the same time, the guardrails must still protect against inappropriate inputs and outputs in other contexts. Guardrails also provide contextual grounding checks that keep model responses factually anchored to the email content being analyzed, reducing false positives caused by model hallucination. This allows the AI-powered analysis to operate within defined boundaries while still detecting intricate patterns.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, you will learn how to implement a multi-stage email analysis pipeline using Amazon Bedrock foundation models that evaluate sender behavior patterns, contextual appropriateness, and communication anomalies to identify AI-generated phishing attempts before they reach your users.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="implementation-framework"&gt;Implementation framework&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following framework shows how to put this into practice within your existing email security infrastructure, so that someone in John’s position can move from reactive filtering to proactive detection. After your standard authentication checks (SPF, DKIM, DMARC) confirm an email comes from a legitimate mail server, the phishing detection workflow goes a step further by layering in behavioral analysis. Your system moves from checking whether a server is authorized to evaluating whether a message matches how your coworker normally communicates.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/22/ML-19725-1-2.png" alt="Email security analysis workflow with five steps: input guardrails and pre-processing, prompt construction with context, AI-powered analysis with guardrails, multi-factor risk scoring, and classification and automated routing" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Figure 1 maps the five-step email security analysis workflow, from initial guardrail screening through AI analysis, risk scoring, and final routing decisions.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Before diving into the implementation, let’s clarify what each component does. Behavioral analysis starts with a sender baseline tracker, which is a profile of each person who sends email to you. The sender baseline tracker logs how your employees normally write, so the Amazon Bedrock analysis pipeline has a reference point to compare against.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Over continued use, the phishing detection workflow will understand the words your employees use, how formal or casual they are, what they usually ask for, and who they normally communicate with. Consider John’s environment: A coworker who usually sends quick one-liners suddenly writes a formal email requesting an urgent wire transfer. The analysis pipeline catches that shift and flags it for John’s team to take a closer look.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This can help reduce false alarms and save time that John’s team might otherwise spend sorting through flagged email messages that turn out not to be real threats.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Here’s a high-level outline on how these components work together when an email enters your phishing detection workflow:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-1-input-guardrails-and-pre-processing"&gt;Step 1: Input guardrails and pre-processing&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;INITIALIZE EmailSecurityAnalyzer:
    - Set up Amazon Bedrock client (Claude Sonnet 4.5 model)
    - Configure Amazon Bedrock Guardrails for PII protection and content filtering
    - Initialize knowledge base for phishing examples
    - Initialize sender baseline tracker
    - Set risk thresholds (safe &amp;lt; 30, suspicious &amp;lt; 70, dangerous &amp;gt;= 70)

FUNCTION analyze_email(email):
    // Step 1: Pre-process with guardrails
    processed_email = apply_input_guardrails(email)
    IF content_blocked:
        RETURN manual_review_required&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The phishing detection workflow first runs incoming email messages through Amazon Bedrock Guardrails, which screen for sensitive content and flag anything that should go to manual review before the analysis begins.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-2-prompt-construction-with-context"&gt;Step 2: Prompt construction with context&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;// Step 2: Build analysis prompt
prompt = construct_prompt(
    email_content,
    sender_baseline_patterns,
    organizational_context,
    known_phishing_examples
)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After an email clears that check, the workflow constructs an analysis prompt by combining the email’s content with the sender’s baseline communication patterns, organizational context, and known phishing examples by using &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/knowledge-bases/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock Knowledge Bases&lt;/a&gt;. That way, the model is evaluating the message against a full picture, not in a vacuum.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-3-ai-powered-analysis-with-guardrails"&gt;Step 3: AI-powered analysis with guardrails&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;// Step 3: Invoke AI model with guardrails
analysis = bedrock_invoke_with_guardrails(prompt)
IF guardrail_intervened:
    RETURN blocked_with_reasons&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The foundation model processes the email using the constructed prompt while guardrails keep the analysis within your defined security boundaries. The foundation model can examine suspicious content thoroughly while the guardrails keep it from generating outputs that expose sensitive information in the process.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-4-multi-factor-risk-scoring"&gt;Step 4: Multi-factor risk scoring&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;// Step 4: Calculate risk scores
risk_score = weighted_average(
    content_anomaly_score,
    behavioral_deviation_score,
    context_alignment_score
)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;From that analysis, the Amazon Bedrock pipeline generates three scores: one for content anomalies, one for behavioral deviations, and one for contextual alignment. The pipeline combines them into a single risk score from 0–100, which determines where the email is routed.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-5-classification-and-automated-routing"&gt;Step 5: Classification and automated routing&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;// Step 5: Classify and route
risk_level = classify_risk(risk_score)
action = route_email(risk_level) // DELIVER, QUARANTINE, or BLOCK
RETURN analysis_result

FUNCTION route_email(risk_level):
    IF risk_level == SAFE: deliver_to_inbox
    IF risk_level == SUSPICIOUS: quarantine_for_review
    IF risk_level == DANGEROUS: block_and_alert_security&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Safe messages land in your employees’ inboxes as usual. Suspicious email messages get quarantined for your security team to review. Dangerous messages are blocked outright.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="continuous-learning-through-feedback"&gt;Continuous learning through feedback&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;FUNCTION process_feedback(email, is_phishing):
    IF is_phishing:
        add_to_phishing_knowledge_base(email)
    ELSE:
        update_sender_baseline(email)
        add_to_legitimate_examples(email)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;These steps happen in milliseconds as messages move through your routing system. Your existing infrastructure still handles message routing and delivery. The analysis runs alongside it as an inspection layer that evaluates behavioral risk before messages reach your users’ inboxes.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Over continued use, the phishing detection workflow improves its accuracy in making these calls through a few complementary techniques. Dynamic prompt engineering, the practice of iteratively refining the instructions sent to the foundation model based on real-world results, takes feedback from the security team and incorporates it directly into your analysis prompts, gradually fine-tuning how the model evaluates potential issues. That feedback loop also feeds into a growing knowledge base of validated examples, where confirmed phishing attempts and legitimate messages are cataloged and later used as few-shot learning demonstrations in future prompts. So, when a new email comes in, the model isn’t working from scratch. It references your real, previously verified examples that match similar patterns to make a more informed judgment.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="example-ai-generated-phishing-email-analysis"&gt;Example: AI-generated phishing email analysis&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following AI-generated phishing email message demonstrates modern phishing sophistication. Notice the perfect grammar, legitimate business context, and reference to a real purchase order (PO) format. None of these would trigger traditional spam filters. Following the email message is a simplified prompt structure showing how Amazon Bedrock analyzes messages against sender baselines and known phishing patterns. The prompt combines email content with historical context to support behavioral analysis beyond surface-level filtering. Last is a sample risk assessment output identifying a vendor impersonation attempt. The Amazon Bedrock pipeline flagged behavioral anomalies, including a first-ever payment change request, along with domain inconsistencies that traditional authentication checks missed.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="sample-phishing-email"&gt;Sample phishing email&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;Hi Sarah,

Following up on our last call Tuesday about the Q3 reconciliation.

Our finance team has updated our banking details as part of our transition to Example Banking Inc.

Could you update the payment info for PO-2024-089? Before the November 15th deadline? New details attached.

Best,
Michael Chen | Example Inc.&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="prompt-structure-and-risk-assessment-output"&gt;Prompt structure and risk assessment output&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;=== EMAIL CONTENT ===
{email_content}

=== SENDER BASELINE ===
- Domain: example.com (verified vendor)
- History: 2-3 emails/month, never requested payment changes
- Tone: Professional, invoice/contract discussions

=== KNOWN EVENT PATTERNS ===
- Vendor impersonation with lookalike domains
- Payment detail change requests referencing valid POs risk assessment

=== Task ===
Score (0-100): content anomalies, behavioral deviation, context alignment

{
    "risk_score": 78,
    "risk_level": "DANGEROUS",
    "key_findings": [
        "Domain mismatch: 'example-website.com' vs 'example.com'",
        "First-ever payment change request from this sender",
        "Phone number doesn't match vendor records"
    ]
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="the-continuous-feedback-loop"&gt;The continuous feedback loop&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Behind these examples, the phishing detection system maintains dynamic sender baselines in a database that tracks each of your sender’s typical communication patterns, vocabulary, tone, and request types. False positives flagged by John’s security team are fed back into the phishing detection pipeline, updating baselines to account for legitimate variations in how senders communicate. Confirmed phishing patterns are cataloged alongside these baselines to enrich future prompt context with current intelligence. The result is a feedback loop where every correction and every confirmed threat make the analysis more accurate.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/22/ML-19725-2-2.png" alt="Continuous feedback loop diagram showing the five stages arranged as a cycle: analyze, score, review, learn, and enhance, with arrows connecting each stage to the next" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The continuous feedback pipeline runs across five stages:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;blockquote&gt;
 &lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;1. Analyze&lt;/strong&gt; – The foundation model evaluates your incoming email messages using dynamic prompts built from accumulated phishing attempt intelligence and sender context.&lt;/p&gt; 
 &lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;2. Score&lt;/strong&gt; – Based on that analysis, a risk score from 0–100 is assigned, and suspicious messages are quarantined for your security team’s review.&lt;/p&gt; 
 &lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;3. Review&lt;/strong&gt; – Flagged messages get classified as either a confirmed phishing attempt or a false positive.&lt;/p&gt; 
 &lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;4. Learn&lt;/strong&gt; – Those classifications feed back into your system, updating the example library, sender behavior baselines, and emerging patterns catalog.&lt;/p&gt; 
 &lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;5. Enhance&lt;/strong&gt; – New examples and confirmed phishing attempt patterns get incorporated into the analysis prompts, improving detection accuracy for the next cycle.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/blockquote&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Early cycles will require more hands-on review as your system creates its baseline understanding. For John, that means his team initially spends more time classifying flagged messages, but the investment pays off quickly. As the example library and sender profiles grow, the model becomes progressively more accurate at distinguishing legitimate communications from phishing attempts. John stays in the loop throughout, but his attention shifts from sifting through noise to focusing on genuinely suspicious messages.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Each cycle through this loop creates a stronger, more adaptive defense that evolves alongside the phishing attempts it was designed to catch. That continuous improvement is what separates this feedback-driven detection model from static, signature-based detection.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Phishing detection can no longer rely on surface-level indicators such as typos and awkward phrasing. The framework in this post addresses that shift by combining the Amazon Bedrock foundation models with behavioral analysis, contextual grounding, and a continuous feedback loop that improves accuracy over time. Amazon Bedrock catches subtle manipulation attempts that trained eyes might miss, while your existing infrastructure keeps doing what it was built to do.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Pair these defenses with solid verification processes, healthy skepticism toward unexpected requests, and a security culture that keeps your teams moving confidently. Employee awareness still matters, but now generative AI works with you to identify and help prevent impersonation attempts. AI made phishing harder to detect. The same technology, applied defensively, makes it harder to succeed.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To begin implementing these defenses, start by visiting the &lt;a href="https://console.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock console&lt;/a&gt;. You can configure Amazon Bedrock Guardrails for your email flow and follow this tutorial to build your own email phishing detection pipeline. Share your experience with AI-powered security in the comments.&lt;/p&gt;

		<item>
		<title>Best practices for multi-turn reinforcement learning in Amazon SageMaker AI</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/best-practices-for-multi-turn-reinforcement-learning-in-amazon-sagemaker-ai/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Sapana Chaudhary]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Thu, 02 Jul 2026 17:50:23 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Advanced (300)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon SageMaker AI]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">7e400a6b907735c3f5b62e1203c543bf320b85bc</guid>

					<description>In this post, we share best practices for reliable multi-turn RL training. We cover how to build a training environment you can trust, set up an external evaluation, design a reward aligned with the end task, manage what changes once the agent runs for multiple turns, and monitor the metrics that tell you when to iterate.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;Training a multi-turn agent in Amazon SageMaker AI to resolve support tickets or moderate content means handling a sequence of dependent steps, not a single response. These agents read instructions, make tool calls, read the results, decide the next action, and recover from a mistake before committing to an answer. That flexibility is also what makes agentic reinforcement learning (RL) challenging. More ways to act mean more ways to satisfy the reward without doing the task, and the environment the agent trains against can quietly corrupt the training signal.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we share best practices for reliable multi-turn RL training. We cover how to build a training environment you can trust, set up an external evaluation, design a reward aligned with the end task, manage what changes once the agent runs for multiple turns, and monitor the metrics that tell you when to iterate. We draw our examples from the &lt;a href="https://github.com/amazon-science/SOP-Bench" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;SOP-Bench&lt;/a&gt; dataset, an Amazon Science benchmark that evaluates agents’ ability to resolve tasks based on complex Standard Operating Procedures (SOP) across 12 business domains.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="sagemaker-ai-multi-turn-reinforcement-learning"&gt;SageMaker AI multi-turn reinforcement learning&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/about-aws/whats-new/2026/06/multi-turn-reinforcement-learning-on-sagemaker-ai/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker AI multi-turn RL&lt;/a&gt; (SageMaker AI MTRL) provides the training loop for agentic tasks. Your agent can run on &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/agentcore/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock AgentCore&lt;/a&gt;, Amazon Elastic Kubernetes Service (Amazon EKS), Amazon Elastic Compute Cloud (Amazon EC2), AWS Fargate, or infrastructure of your choice. You connect it through a small adapter that exposes your tool surface to the rollout server, and SageMaker AI MTRL handles the rest:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;A modular agent-environment interface&lt;/strong&gt; that keeps integration low-code while giving you full algorithmic control. Custom rewards, custom tool loops, and multi-turn conversation shapes are all yours to define.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Serverless execution&lt;/strong&gt; that simplifies infrastructure concerns, so you get production-scale agentic RL at per-token pricing without provisioning or managing GPU clusters.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Asynchronous rollout and trajectory collection&lt;/strong&gt; with bounded off-policy staleness. Generation and gradient updates run in parallel without drifting too far from the current policy, which speeds up training.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;A native algorithm library&lt;/strong&gt; spanning Proximal Policy Optimization (PPO), Clipped Importance Sampling Policy Optimization (CISPO), and importance-sampling (IS) losses, paired with multiple group-based advantage estimators (GRPO, GRPO &lt;code&gt;pass@k&lt;/code&gt;, RLOO, and more). These cover the choices most relevant to multi-turn agentic RL.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Sequence-extension training&lt;/strong&gt; to keep wall-clock down on long multi-turn trajectories.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Trajectory and reward observability&lt;/strong&gt; in &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/sagemaker-ai/experiments/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;MLflow managed by Amazon SageMaker AI&lt;/a&gt;, so you can read what your agent did turn by turn, and across training steps.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Evaluation jobs&lt;/strong&gt; report reward, &lt;code&gt;pass@k&lt;/code&gt;, trajectory metrics, and more before you deploy to a SageMaker AI endpoint or Amazon Bedrock.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The service provides the training loop, hardware, and orchestration. The choices that decide whether you get a reliable agent are yours. You build the environment the agent trains against, measure success outside the reward, design the reward itself, and decide how to iterate when the curve stalls.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/29/ML-21260-1.jpg" alt="Architecture overview of the SageMaker AI multi-turn RL service showing the agent, rollout server, and training loop" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Figure 1: Overview of the SageMaker AI multi-turn RL service&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="build-a-training-environment-that-is-cheap-reproducible-and-representative"&gt;Build a training environment that is cheap, reproducible, and representative&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Single-turn RL needs a prompt and a reward function. Multi-turn RL adds an environment for the agent to act in across turns: the tools it calls and the systems behind them. That environment is part of your training setup, and the way you build it shapes both what the model can learn and whether you can trust your metrics.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When training an agent, build a sandboxed or simulated environment that resembles production but stays isolated from live traffic. Tool calls and responses keep the same schemas and business logic. They are driven by recorded responses or isolated state instead of live calls.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Simulated environments are the recommended starting point because a typical run produces many thousands of rollouts, each making several tool calls. As an example, a batch size of 128 with group size 8 is 1,024 rollouts per step. Pointing that traffic at live systems can lead to customer impact. Without a simulated environment, exploration can produce real side effects. For example, an agent learning by trial and error will issue refunds, delete records, or trigger workflows that you didn’t intend. Additionally, live data shifts under you, so the same trajectory scores differently across runs. You must know the correct outcome to compute a reward, which means a fixed, labeled set of tasks (or a trustworthy judge model) regardless of where the tool calls go.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;How you build the simulated environment depends on what your tools do. Three patterns cover most use-cases you will encounter:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Read-only tools: Replay recorded responses keyed by their inputs.&lt;/strong&gt; These tools help the agent retrieve information relevant to a task. For example, in SOP-Bench the customer service task provides ten mocked tools (&lt;code&gt;validateAccount&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;getAuthenticationDetails&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;createSessionAndOpenTicket&lt;/code&gt;, and so on), each returning a deterministic response from a fixture, such as a specific row from a CSV file based on the tool call arguments.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Stateful tools: Seeded sandboxes that hold state for the length of an episode.&lt;/strong&gt; When the agent writes something and reads it back, the environment needs memory. The pattern: allocate per-episode resources at the start of the rollout, and register everything the agent creates. Tear it all down in a &lt;code&gt;try/finally&lt;/code&gt; block when the episode ends, whether by reaching a terminal action, hitting &lt;code&gt;max_turns&lt;/code&gt;, or crashing. No state leaks into the next rollout.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Verifiable outcomes: Genuine execution in an isolated simulation environment.&lt;/strong&gt; When the agent’s output is code, SQL, or math, you can run it in an isolated environment. Use a Docker &lt;code&gt;exec&lt;/code&gt; for code, an in-memory SQLite per rollout for SQL, a pure Python eval for math. Real execution, deterministic per-instance, same input plus same sandbox state equals same result. For example, &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock-agentcore/latest/devguide/code-interpreter-tool.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AgentCore Code Interpreter&lt;/a&gt; provides managed isolated environments for code execution.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Whichever pattern fits, hold two properties fixed:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Reproducibility&lt;/strong&gt;: the same tool called with the same arguments returns the same result, so the reward for an identical trajectory is stable and your evaluation is comparable across runs.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Representativeness&lt;/strong&gt;: build the environment from your real schemas and data distributions so the behavior the model learns transfers to production.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Before you start training, confirm your environment is configured correctly:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul class="task-list"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;label&gt;Tool calls with the same arguments give the same result, verified by running the same instance twice and diffing the rollout messages.&lt;/label&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;label&gt;Per-rollout state is isolated (separate temp directory, separate IDs, separate DB connection).&lt;/label&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;label&gt;Available tools match your production environment, along with tool request/response schemas.&lt;/label&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="set-up-an-external-evaluation-before-you-train"&gt;Set up an external evaluation before you train&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After your environment is in place and verified, build a way to measure success before you write a reward function. That measure should capture your end goal directly. RL optimizes the reward signal literally, so if the reward is the only number you watch, you cannot separate progress on the task from progress on satisfying the reward criteria. You need an external evaluation you can trust to guide your decisions while you iterate on rewards, environment seeding, and hyperparameters.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="pattern"&gt;Pattern&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Stand up a held-out evaluation that scores the outcome you care about at deployment, computed independently of the reward. In practice this is a small piece of code that takes a model, runs it through the rollout server on a fixed test split, and returns a single task-success rate. It can be minimal, as long as it is honest.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For SOP-Bench, the evaluation is exact-match on the final JSON object inside &lt;code&gt;&amp;lt;final_output&amp;gt;&lt;/code&gt;: every field in the agent’s output has to match the ground-truth field, or the rollout scores zero. The reward function can compute partial credit and weighted components. The evaluation doesn’t.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Before any training, establish a baseline. Run the base model and a reference model (a frontier model hosted on Amazon Bedrock is a good fit) through the same evaluation. This tells you two things: how far the base model has to go, and what &lt;em&gt;good&lt;/em&gt; looks like on this task.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="anti-pattern"&gt;Anti-pattern&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Treating the training reward, or a metric derived from it, as your measure of success. This might seem intuitive, but to capture reward hacking, you need external evaluation. Multi-turn agents need special consideration: a reward that pays out for tool calls teaches the agent to call as many tools as it can. A reward that penalizes turn count teaches the agent to commit to an answer before it has the information it needs. Either way, the training reward rises but the agent’s real success at its task falls.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Before you start training, confirm your evaluation is trustworthy:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul class="task-list"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;label&gt;The evaluation is one function, &lt;code&gt;score(rollout) -&amp;gt; float&lt;/code&gt;, scoring exactly what you ship.&lt;/label&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;label&gt;Baseline evaluation is non-zero on the base model you plan to fine-tune (if it’s zero, see &lt;em&gt;Make sure the base model has a foothold first&lt;/em&gt; in the next section).&lt;/label&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;label&gt;Run your evaluation against a frontier model so you have an advanced baseline to compare against.&lt;/label&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="design-a-good-multi-turn-rl-reward-function"&gt;Design a good multi-turn RL reward function&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Reward design is one of the more challenging open problems in RL. The same flexibility that lets the agent solve a real task lets it find ways to satisfy the reward without doing the task. Every component you add, every reward weight you tune, every formatting bonus you layer in is another surface where the agent can climb without solving the task. The model optimizes what you wrote down, not what you meant. By default use the same scoring rule for training and evaluation, and only deviate when you have a concrete reason.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Take SOP-Bench. The benchmark expects the answer as a JSON object inside &lt;code&gt;&amp;lt;final_output&amp;gt;&lt;/code&gt; tags:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{
  "aircraft_ready": "true",
  "mechanical_inspection_result": "success",
  "electrical_inspection_result": "success",
  "component_incident_response": "success",
  "component_mismatch_response": "success",
  "cross_check_reporting_response": "success"
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The benchmark scores 1 if every field matches and 0 otherwise. Training and evaluation usually share this scoring rule and differ only in what you observe around it. The trainer consumes one reward (scalar or list of scalars) per rollout. Evaluation runs at lower frequency on a fixed split, so you can monitor more metrics: per-field accuracy, completion rate (did the agent emit &lt;code&gt;&amp;lt;final_output&amp;gt;&lt;/code&gt; at all), tool-call distribution, turn budget exhaustion, format compliance.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;There are two real reasons to deviate from the default benchmark scoring rule, and both call for a denser reward.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The first is algorithmic. RL computes the learning signal from variance across a group of &lt;code&gt;group_size&lt;/code&gt; rollouts per prompt, using a group-based advantage method (&lt;code&gt;advantage_method&lt;/code&gt;). The service default &lt;code&gt;group_based&lt;/code&gt; is GRPO. Many other methods like &lt;code&gt;rloo&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;grpo_passk&lt;/code&gt; are also available. See the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/model-customize-mtrl-hyperparams.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;documentation&lt;/a&gt; for a full list. A binary score can collapse that variance: when every rollout in a group scores the same, the relative signal is zero and the group contributes no gradient. When &lt;code&gt;rollout/reward/valid_mean&lt;/code&gt; (the mean over non-zero-advantage groups) drifts below &lt;code&gt;rollout/reward/mean&lt;/code&gt; and the model stalls, that gap is the symptom.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The second is convergence speed. Even when group variance is healthy, a dense reward gives the model gradient toward partial progress on every rollout, not only the ones that fully succeed. A rollout that gets five of six fields right teaches the model what &lt;em&gt;closer&lt;/em&gt; looks like. A binary score teaches it nothing about that.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A dense reward for the SOP-Bench task scores each field independently and returns a reward &lt;strong&gt;scalar or list of scalars&lt;/strong&gt; (per-turn rewards) plus a &lt;strong&gt;metrics dictionary&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;class SOPBenchReward:
    """Dense per-field reward for the SOP-Bench aircraft-inspection task.
    Returns a scalar in [0, 1] plus a metrics dict surfaced in MLflow."""
    ground_truth: dict[str, str]
    format_coef: float = 0.1            # format is a small shaping term, not the objective

    async def __call__(self, history: list[Message]) -&amp;gt; tuple[float, dict[str, float]]:
        fields = parse_final_output(last_assistant(history))   # JSON inside &amp;lt;final_output&amp;gt;
        emitted = float(fields is not None)
        if fields is None:                                     # no parseable answer
            return self.format_coef * (emitted - 1), {"completion": 0.0, "field_acc": 0.0}
        matched = sum(1 for k, v in self.ground_truth.items()
                      if str(fields.get(k)).strip().lower() == str(v).strip().lower())
        field_acc = matched / len(self.ground_truth)           # partial credit: 5/6 &amp;gt; 0
        reward = field_acc + self.format_coef * (emitted - 1)  # correctness dominates
        return reward, {"completion": emitted, "field_acc": field_acc}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Your agent reports the reward through &lt;code&gt;update_reward&lt;/code&gt;, and the metrics dictionary (&lt;code&gt;completion&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;field_acc&lt;/code&gt;) appears in MLflow. To credit individual turns instead of the whole trajectory, &lt;code&gt;update_reward&lt;/code&gt; also accepts a per-turn list, paired with the &lt;code&gt;group_based_per_turn&lt;/code&gt; advantage method, so your reward function can also return one reward value per turn.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Verify the reward on real outputs before you train on it.&lt;/strong&gt; A reward parser more forgiving than your evaluation is its own kind of reward hack. In one of our SOP-Bench runs the reward accepted a looser output format than the benchmark scored: a bare &lt;code&gt;&amp;lt;final_response&amp;gt;&lt;/code&gt; wrapper earned credit even though the benchmark only reads &lt;code&gt;&amp;lt;final_output&amp;gt;&lt;/code&gt;. Training did exactly what we asked: the model learned to drop the tag the benchmark needed, the reward climbed, but the external evaluation fell.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Make sure the base model has a foothold first.&lt;/strong&gt; RL improves what the base model can already do some fraction of the time. It doesn’t invent capability from nothing. If the base model produces zero successful trajectories on your task, the reward signal has nothing to amplify and training stalls.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;SageMaker AI MTRL can run such a baseline as a managed evaluation job. &lt;code&gt;MultiTurnRLEvaluator&lt;/code&gt; replays your agent over a &lt;strong&gt;held-out prompt set&lt;/strong&gt; and reports &lt;code&gt;eval/reward&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;pass@k&lt;/code&gt;. If you have already trained a model, a single call with &lt;code&gt;evaluate_base_model=True&lt;/code&gt; scores the base and fine-tuned model side by side. Because &lt;code&gt;pass@k&lt;/code&gt; thresholds the reward at &lt;code&gt;success_threshold&lt;/code&gt;, setting &lt;code&gt;success_threshold=1&lt;/code&gt; gives you a strict success rate: the fraction of rollouts that scored a perfect reward alongside the mean.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;from sagemaker.train.evaluate import MultiTurnRLEvaluator

# With Bedrock AgentCore
evaluator_base = MultiTurnRLEvaluator(
    model="openai-reasoning-gpt-oss-20b",
    dataset="s3://my-bucket/eval-prompts.parquet",
    agent_config="arn:aws:bedrock-agentcore:us-west-2:123456789012:runtime/my-agent",
    s3_output_path="s3://my-bucket/eval-output/base/",
    mlflow_resource_arn="arn:aws:sagemaker:us-west-2:123456789012:mlflow-tracking-server/my-mlflow",
    role="arn:aws:iam::123456789012:role/SageMakerRole",
    accept_eula=True,
)

execution = evaluator_base.evaluate()
execution.wait()&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In the specified &lt;code&gt;s3_output_path&lt;/code&gt;, you will find the reported metrics of the evaluation which you can also review in MLflow, along with evaluation trajectories. For reward-based evaluation of fine-tuned and base models, see the documentation on &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/model-customize-mtrl-evaluation.html"&gt;Model evaluation&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Keep one distinction in mind: the evaluation job scores rollouts with your agent’s own reward function, so it measures held-out generalization, &lt;em&gt;not independence from the reward&lt;/em&gt;. A lenient reward parser would look healthy here, because the metric is the reward itself. The independent check that catches reward-parser bugs stays separate: score the same rollouts with a stricter, independent parser (for SOP-Bench, the benchmark’s exact-match scorer) and compare. You can even run that strict scorer as its own evaluation job by pointing &lt;code&gt;MultiTurnRLEvaluator&lt;/code&gt; at an agent whose reward is the independent metric.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For a deeper treatment of reward design, sparse vs.&amp;nbsp;dense rewards, judge models, multi-objective shaping, and the trade-offs between them, see the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/model-customize-mtrl-assets.html#model-customize-mtrl-assets-best-practices" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;SageMaker AI reward design best practices&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Before you trust your reward, confirm:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul class="task-list"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;label&gt;Training reward and evaluation share the same underlying scoring rule unless you have a measured reason to diverge (and that reason is documented).&lt;/label&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;label&gt;Reward returns a float in &lt;code&gt;[0, 1]&lt;/code&gt; (or &lt;code&gt;[-1, 1]&lt;/code&gt; if you allow negative regression terms).&lt;/label&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;label&gt;Reward over 100 baseline rollouts has variance (not all 0, not all 1). If it doesn’t, that’s the kind of measured signal that justifies either shaping or devising a dedicated data curriculum.&lt;/label&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;label&gt;No baseline rollout scores higher on the training reward than on the eval. If it does, the reward is over-rewarding something the external eval doesn’t credit.&lt;/label&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;label&gt;If the reward has multiple components, verify you log each separately in MLflow so you can read divergence per term.&lt;/label&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="manage-what-changes-when-the-agent-runs-for-multiple-turns"&gt;Manage what changes when the agent runs for multiple turns&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A multi-turn agent has to manage concerns single-turn doesn’t see. These are worth designing for explicitly before you start training.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Context grows every turn, and turn budgets are part of the reward design.&lt;/strong&gt; Each tool call extends the conversation: the call, its arguments, the result, and the reasoning the model produces between them. Long trajectories accumulate context fast, and MTRL uses sequence-extension training to keep wall-clock manageable as they grow. A task that needs eight calls in sequence might run out of room before it finishes. Two budgets bound this: &lt;code&gt;max_turns&lt;/code&gt;, which your agent loop controls, and the per-turn token budget, which the service sets through &lt;code&gt;sampling_max_tokens&lt;/code&gt; (rollout) and &lt;code&gt;val_sampling_params.sampling_max_tokens&lt;/code&gt; (evaluation). Pick both to match what your task needs and what you can afford to serve at deployment.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For SOP-Bench, eight turns and a 2,048-token per-turn budget cover the canonical procedure with margin to spare (&lt;code&gt;sampling_max_tokens&lt;/code&gt; allows up to 8,192). A rule of thumb: if a human walkthrough of the task takes N turns, set &lt;code&gt;max_turns = ceil(N * 1.5)&lt;/code&gt; in your agent loop. The right turn budget is the smallest one that lets the agent finish with a small safety margin. Watch &lt;code&gt;rollout/tokens/response_max&lt;/code&gt; for responses clustering at the cap. If more than 5 percent of rollouts hit it, raise &lt;code&gt;sampling_max_tokens&lt;/code&gt;. That signal is silent loss otherwise. The model learns from a truncated trajectory but does not see the reward it would have earned by finishing.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="separate-completion-from-correctness"&gt;Separate completion from correctness&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A trajectory that finishes with the wrong answer and one that never finishes are different failures, and conflating them hides where the model is breaking. The &lt;code&gt;rollout&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;val&lt;/code&gt; metric families in MLflow give you both signals separately:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Metric&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;What it tells you&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;1&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;rollout/reward/mean&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Average trajectory reward, your training-side signal&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;2&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;rollout/reward/zero_frac&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Fraction of trajectories that scored exactly 0&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;3&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;rollout/turns/mean&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Average turns per trajectory&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;4&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;analysis/zero_adv_groups&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Groups where every rollout scored the same, wasting rollouts&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;5&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;val/reward/mean&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Mean validation reward your held-out data signal&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;6&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;val/reward/pass_k_1&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;pass_k_8&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;pass@1 and &lt;code&gt;pass@k&lt;/code&gt; on the held-out set&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A high &lt;code&gt;val/reward/pass_k_1&lt;/code&gt; on a low completion rate (rollouts hitting &lt;code&gt;max_turns&lt;/code&gt; before emitting a &lt;code&gt;&amp;lt;final_output&amp;gt;&lt;/code&gt;) means the model gets the easy paths right and stalls on the hard ones, suggesting turn-budget tuning. A high completion rate on a low &lt;code&gt;val/reward/pass_k_1&lt;/code&gt; means it answers fluently but wrong, suggesting reward redesign. The two failure modes call for different fixes, so it is worth telling them apart.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Before you commit a turn budget, confirm:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul class="task-list"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;label&gt;&lt;code&gt;max_turns&lt;/code&gt; in your agent loop is calibrated to the task, not left at an arbitrary default.&lt;/label&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;label&gt;Less than 5 percent of training rollouts hit &lt;code&gt;sampling_max_tokens&lt;/code&gt; on any single turn.&lt;/label&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;label&gt;Less than 10 percent of training rollouts hit &lt;code&gt;max_turns&lt;/code&gt; without producing a final answer.&lt;/label&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;label&gt;Completion (final answer emitted) and correctness (final answer right) are tracked as separate metrics in MLflow.&lt;/label&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="monitor-training-metrics"&gt;Monitor training metrics&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After you’ve set up and verified your evaluation, environment, and reward, it’s time to start training. SageMaker AI MTRL provides the high-level &lt;code&gt;MultiTurnRLTrainer&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;MultiTurnRLEvaluator&lt;/code&gt; constructs to train and score your agent:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;from sagemaker.train import MultiTurnRLTrainer
from sagemaker.train.evaluate import MultiTurnRLEvaluator

trainer = MultiTurnRLTrainer(recipe="&amp;lt;per-model starter recipe&amp;gt;", role=..., dataset=...)
trainer.train()                                  # step 6: watch rollout/reward and completion in MLflow

evaluator = MultiTurnRLEvaluator(model=trainer, dataset="&amp;lt;held-out split&amp;gt;",
                                 evaluate_base_model=True)   # step 7: val/reward + pass@k, base vs fine-tuned
evaluator.evaluate().wait()
print(trainer.get_mlflow_url())                  # read the trajectories where reward and evaluation disagree&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;While training, watch &lt;code&gt;rollout/reward/mean&lt;/code&gt; next to the completion rate and open a few trajectories in MLflow (under the &lt;strong&gt;Traces&lt;/strong&gt; tab), so a reward that rises on flat completion doesn’t slip past. The signal that matters at evaluation is disagreement: when &lt;code&gt;rollout/reward/mean&lt;/code&gt; climbs but &lt;code&gt;val/reward/mean&lt;/code&gt; stays flat, the reward is being hacked. Open those trajectories and compare what the reward credited against what the evaluation scored. That comparison drives your reward design iteration: tighten the reward parser, reshape a component, or curate the data, then run again. Each iteration is faster than the last because the environment and evaluation stay fixed. Only the reward and the data change, and MTRL’s per-model starter recipes give you a tuned point to start from.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For example, in one of our earliest attempts we were trying to train an agent on all SOP-Bench tasks at the same time, which led to tasks competing and reward fluctuating:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/29/ML-21260-2.jpg" alt="Training reward curve fluctuating when all SOP-Bench tasks are trained together" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Figure 2: Reward fluctuating when trying to train all SOP-Bench tasks together&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After limiting our data to focus on a single task (&lt;code&gt;aircraft_inspection&lt;/code&gt;), we noticed validation reward going down while rollout reward had saturated. In our reward formulation the max reward was 5.0, but reward had stalled around 3.7:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/29/ML-21260-3.jpg" alt="Reward curve stalling around 3.7 while validation reward drops" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Figure 3: Reward stalling and validation reward dropping&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The model wasn’t earning full reward on &lt;code&gt;aircraft_inspection&lt;/code&gt;, and the Task Success Rate on the external benchmark went &lt;em&gt;down&lt;/em&gt; for the fine-tuned model compared to the base model. We needed to review rollout trajectories to find out why. The SOP’s one-shot example didn’t match the task’s ground-truth data in two ways. It omitted the &lt;code&gt;cross_check_response&lt;/code&gt; field that the data required, so the model couldn’t produce a complete answer, and it wrapped the output in a different tag than the evaluation expected. We aligned the example with the data and dropped the unanswerable field, which let the reward and the evaluation measure the same thing.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/29/ML-21260-4.jpg" alt="Healthy rising reward and validation reward curves for the aircraft_inspection task" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Figure 4: Healthy reward signals for the aircraft_inspection task of SOP-Bench&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When measuring the Task Success Rate (TSR) of a fine-tuned GPT-OSS 20B model against the external benchmark, we saw TSR increase by 13 percent and per-field accuracy grow by approximately 16 percent on the &lt;code&gt;aircraft_inspection&lt;/code&gt; task, confirming that our reward function aligns with our external evaluation.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="putting-it-together-an-iteration-loop"&gt;Putting it together: An iteration loop&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The pieces described earlier add up to a single training loop, run in the order they were introduced. You build the environment and the evaluation first, because they are the fixed scaffolding every later step depends on. You then design the reward against that evaluation, and only after that do you train and read the metrics. Keeping the early pieces fixed is what makes each pass fast, so most of your effort goes into the reward and the data. A version that has worked well for us:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Collect representative task data and split into train, validation, and held-out test sets.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Build the training environment from production schemas: hermetic, seeded, reproducible.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Stand up the external evaluation against the test set, computed independently of the reward.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Establish a baseline by running the base model and a frontier reference model through the evaluation. If the base model scores zero, stop and simplify before continuing.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Design the reward, then validate it on real model outputs from the baseline before any training has happened.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Train, monitoring rollout/reward, completion rate, and a sample of trajectories to know what your model is producing during training.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Evaluate the trained model with the external evaluation. Read trajectories, especially the ones where the reward and the evaluation disagree.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Adjust the reward, the environment, or the data, and run again.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When the curve stalls or collapses, walk these in order before tuning anything else:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Symptom&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;First thing to change&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Diagnostic to confirm&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;1&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Reward flat from step 0&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Verify model output formats are aligned with reward&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Perform standalone evaluations on different rewards to align format reward with model’s output structure&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;2&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Train reward flat, all groups score the same&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Drop &lt;code&gt;group_size&lt;/code&gt; from 8 to 4 and increase &lt;code&gt;batch_size&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Watch &lt;code&gt;analysis/zero_adv_groups&lt;/code&gt;, should drop&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;3&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Train reward rising but &lt;code&gt;val/reward/mean&lt;/code&gt; flat&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Reward is being hacked. Re-read trajectories, tighten the reward parser&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Re-run the offline reward review against new baseline rollouts&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;4&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Reward collapses (drops to ~0.0) after step 40–80&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Set &lt;code&gt;async_config.max_steps_off_policy = 0&lt;/code&gt;. If on CISPO, switch to PPO with &lt;code&gt;(0.8, 1.2)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Reward should stabilize, even if lower&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;5&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Reward stalls with limited improvement, all knobs healthy&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Double LoRA capacity (&lt;code&gt;lora_rank=64&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;lora_alpha=128&lt;/code&gt;)&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Higher ceiling within 50 steps if there’s room to grow&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Make one change at a time, observing metrics for 25–50 training steps (gradient updates) per decision. In our runs, most failures became identifiable within roughly 30 steps when these parameters are adjusted deliberately.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Your reward quality and your evaluation decide whether training produces a useful agent, much more than the algorithm or the hyperparameters do. The reward is the only signal the model optimizes, and an evaluation kept separate from it is what tells you whether the agent is learning the task or learning the reward. A carefully designed reward and an evaluation that matches the end task can produce a useful agent; without them, even a strong algorithm yields a model that looks good in training and fails in production.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;SageMaker AI multi-turn RL takes care of most of the operational work and complexity of running a distributed agentic RL training, abstracting away the hardware, orchestration, and training engine. With SageMaker AI multi-turn RL, you focus on creating an accurate environment, where &lt;a href="https://strandsagents.com/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Strands Agents&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/agentcore/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AgentCore&lt;/a&gt; can help you transition your production environment to an agentic setup, and focus on the reward design, evaluation, and parameter tuning.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To get started with agentic RL, you can walk through the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws/sagemaker-python-sdk/blob/db63167c994ad85c959bc89fb4641b86c77fe432/v3-examples/model-customization-examples/mtrl_finetuning_example_notebook_v3_prod.ipynb" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;example notebook for MTRL setup&lt;/a&gt;. See the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/model-customize-mtrl.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;SageMaker AI multi-turn RL documentation&lt;/a&gt; for service-level guidance and the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/model-customize-mtrl-assets.html#model-customize-mtrl-assets-best-practices" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;reward design best practices&lt;/a&gt; for a deeper treatment of the reward topic, or &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/overcoming-reward-signal-challenges-verifiable-rewards-based-reinforcement-learning-with-grpo-on-sagemaker-ai/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;this AWS blog post on GRPO with verifiable rewards&lt;/a&gt;. Finally, the &lt;a href="https://github.com/amazon-science/SOP-Bench" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;SOP-Bench paper and dataset&lt;/a&gt; are the source of the running example used here.&lt;/p&gt;
