		<item>
		<title>Simplify model selection in Amazon Bedrock with the open source Model Profiler</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/simplify-model-selection-in-amazon-bedrock-with-the-open-source-model-profiler/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Maria Oliva Calero]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 01 Jul 2026 17:46:54 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Amazon Bedrock]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Simple Storage Service (S3)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[AWS Lambda]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[AWS Step Functions]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Intermediate (200)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Thought Leadership]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">4da1e298b0b090398797e8dfaebee6c37ea1739e</guid>

					<description>The Amazon Bedrock Model Profiler is an open source tool that aggregates model metadata from multiple AWS APIs and external sources into a single, searchable interface. In this post, you’ll learn what the Model Profiler provides, the real-world scenarios it supports, and how to deploy it in your own environment in under five minutes.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;Generative AI adoption is accelerating across industries, and &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock&lt;/a&gt; provides a managed service for building production-ready AI applications. With access to more than 100 foundation models from providers such as Anthropic, OpenAI, Meta, Mistral AI, Cohere, and Amazon, teams have the flexibility to choose the right model for each use case.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;But choice comes with complexity. Comparing models across capabilities, pricing, regional availability, context window limits and throughput isn’t straightforward. That information is scattered across console pages, documentation, and regional API calls. For organizations evaluating models for new workloads, optimizing cost and performance, or migrating from other AI systems, this fragmented discovery process slows experimentation and delays production decisions.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-bedrock-migration-and-modernization-tools/tree/main/bedrock-model-profiler" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock Model Profiler&lt;/a&gt; addresses this gap. This open source tool aggregates model metadata from multiple AWS APIs and external sources into a single, searchable interface. With advanced filtering, side-by-side comparisons, and detailed model cards, teams can explore the full Amazon Bedrock catalog and make informed, data-driven decisions by easing the manual search effort across various documents and model cards.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, you’ll learn what the Model Profiler provides, the real-world scenarios it supports, and how to deploy it in your own environment in under five minutes.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="solution-overview"&gt;Solution overview&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The Model Profiler is a web application that lets you browse, filter, and compare every foundation model available on Amazon Bedrock in one place. Instead of navigating multiple console pages and documentation sites, you get a single interface with model cards, side-by-side comparisons, regional availability maps, and pricing breakdowns updated daily.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-20648-1.png" alt="Model Profiler showing the Model Explorer interface" width="600" height="686"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Model Profiler showing the Model Explorer interface&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Behind the interface, a fully automated serverless pipeline collects and processes data from seven distinct sources: five AWS APIs and two public URLs. This keeps the catalog accurate without manual intervention.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Source&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Type&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Auth&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Data Collected&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Amazon Bedrock ListFoundationModels API&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;AWS API&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;IAM (Sigv4)&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Model specifications, capabilities, modalities, and regional availability across 33 regions&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;AWS Price List API&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;AWS API&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;IAM (Sigv4)&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;On-demand, batch, and reserved-tier pricing across three service codes&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;AWS Service Quotas API&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;AWS API&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;IAM (Sigv4)&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Tokens-per-minute (TPM) limits, requests-per-minute (RPM) quotas, and throughput constraints&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Amazon Bedrock ListInferenceProfiles API&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;AWS API&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;IAM (Sigv4)&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Cross-region inference configurations and geographic scopes&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Amazon Bedrock Mantle API&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;AWS API&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;IAM (Sigv4)&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Mantle inference availability across regions&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;LiteLLM Model Database&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Public URL&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;None&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Token specifications including context window sizes and max output tokens&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;AWS Documentation&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Public URL&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;None&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Model lifecycle status (active, legacy, end-of-life)&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Understanding key quota metrics is essential when evaluating models. Tokens-per-minute (TPM) measures how many tokens you can process per minute. Think of it as your throughput ceiling, where 1,000 tokens equals roughly 750 words of text. Requests-per-minute (RPM) limits how many API calls you can make regardless of size. Both quotas vary by model and Region.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-20648-2.png" alt="Architecture diagram of the Model Profiler data pipeline" width="600" height="1062"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Architecture diagram of the Model Profiler data pipeline&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The Model Profiler runs a fully automated data pipeline orchestrated by AWS Step Functions. Seventeen AWS Lambda functions process data across four phases, using inter-Lambda S3 caching to reduce API calls from approximately 480 to 29 per execution. That represents a 97% cache hit rate. A self-healing agentic system powered by Amazon Bedrock detects data gaps and automatically applies safe configuration fixes. The entire pipeline completes in 8–12 minutes and runs daily at 6 AM UTC.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The schedule is configurable through the Amazon EventBridge rule in the AWS CloudFormation template. You can adjust the cron expression to run at a different time or frequency based on your requirements.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3&gt;Phase 0: Initialization&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The pipeline begins by dynamically discovering which AWS regions currently support Amazon Bedrock. No Region list is hardcoded, so newly launched regions are picked up automatically. It then initializes the execution context (S3 paths, cache keys) and synchronizes configuration between the backend and the React front end, making sure both sides stay consistent without manual intervention.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="phase-1-parallel-collection"&gt;Phase 1: Parallel collection&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Three independent collection branches run simultaneously. The Pricing branch queries the AWS Price List API across three service codes and aggregates results by provider and model. The Models branch fans out to Amazon Bedrock-enabled regions, calling ListFoundationModels in each and deduplicating results into a single canonical model list. The Quotas branch gathers tokens-per-minute and requests-per-minute service limits in parallel.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="phase-2-parallel-enrichment"&gt;Phase 2: Parallel enrichment&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Once raw data is collected, six enrichment steps run concurrently, reading from the cached data rather than re-calling APIs, to link pricing records to models, compute regional availability, determine cross-region inference support, fetch context window sizes, probe the Mantle API, and determine each model’s lifecycle status.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="phase-3-aggregation-and-intelligence"&gt;Phase 3: Aggregation and intelligence&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The final aggregator merges the enrichment data into two production-ready JSON files: bedrock_models.json (the full model catalog with specifications, quotas, availability, and lifecycle status) and bedrock_pricing.json (pricing organized by provider and model). Both files are copied to Amazon Simple Storage Service (Amazon S3) and served through Amazon CloudFront.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Before publication, a gap detection system scans the output for seven types of data quality issues. When gaps exceed configured thresholds, a self-healing agent analyzes the gap report and automatically applies safe fixes. Changes are versioned and backed up, and suggestions that do not meet safety criteria are logged for manual review rather than auto-applied.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="deployment-options"&gt;Deployment options&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The solution offers two deployment options. Local mode runs entirely on your machine using your existing AWS credentials. Run the data collector, start the frontend, and begin exploring with read access to Amazon Bedrock and Pricing APIs. No cloud infrastructure is needed.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;AWS deployment provides a fully serverless architecture with automated daily data refresh. An AWS Step Functions workflow orchestrates AWS Lambda functions that collect fresh data every day at 6 AM UTC, storing results in Amazon S3 and serving them through Amazon CloudFront. The local collector imports the same transformation functions as the Lambda code, maintaining identical output whether you run locally or in production.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="key-features"&gt;Key features&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The Model Profiler provides four core capabilities that streamline model selection: from discovery and comparison to regional planning and tracking your shortlist.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="model-explorer"&gt;Model Explorer&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The Model Explorer is your starting point for discovering Amazon Bedrock models. Browse 120+ foundation models in a searchable, filterable interface. You can narrow results by provider (Anthropic, Meta, Amazon, Mistral AI, Cohere, and 14 others), by capabilities such as vision, code generation, function calling, or embeddings, and by input and output modalities. Modalities describe the types of data a model can process: a text-only model handles written content, while a multimodal model might accept text and images as input and generate text, images, or both as output. Region filters show only models available in your target regions, and a status filter lets you focus on active models or include legacy options.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-20648-3.png" alt="Model Explorer interface showing filter options and model cards" width="600" height="664"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Model Explorer interface showing filter options and model cards&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A consumption options filter lets you refine results by how you want to use a model. In Region provides on-demand inference in a specific AWS Region with per-token pricing. Cross-Region Inference Service (CRIS) routes requests across regions for higher throughput. Batch processes large volumes asynchronously at lower cost, with results delivered to Amazon S3. Mantle provides managed inference endpoints with dedicated capacity and custom configurations.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The explorer supports two view modes. Card view provides visual browsing with key specifications at a glance. Table view enables dense comparison across many models. Full-text search works across model names, descriptions, and provider information.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Every model has a comprehensive detail view consolidating the available information: technical specifications (modalities, context windows, inference types), pricing breakdowns by Region and consumption type, regional availability with cross-region inference details, service quotas per Region (TPM and RPM limits), and links to official documentation and provider information.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-20648-4.png" alt="Model card displaying technical specifications information" width="600" height="1230"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Model card displaying technical specifications information&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="model-comparison"&gt;Model Comparison&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Once you have identified candidate models, the comparison view lets you analyze them across every dimension that matters.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-20648-5.png" alt="Model Comparison view showing four models side-by-side with tabs for Overview and Availability" width="600" height="954"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Model Comparison view showing four models side-by-side with tabs for Overview and Availability&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Compare up to 25 models simultaneously across four dimensions. Pricing covers input and output token costs across regions, with on-demand, batch, and provisioned tiers. Regional availability shows maps of where each model is available, including cross-region inference support. Specifications include context window sizes, maximum output tokens, and supported features. A capabilities matrix shows what each model supports side by side.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon Bedrock offers multiple pricing options to match different workload patterns. On-demand tiers include standard, flex, and priority options with per-token pricing and no commitment. Batch pricing provides discounts ranging from 30-50% depending on the model and modality for non-time-sensitive jobs processed within 24 hours. Reserved tier provides dedicated capacity at a fixed hourly rate for sustained high-volume workloads. For detailed pricing by model, see the &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/pricing/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock Pricing&lt;/a&gt; page.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="regional-availability-matrix"&gt;Regional Availability Matrix&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For teams planning multi-region deployments, the Regional Availability view provides a comprehensive model-by-region matrix across all 33 Amazon Bedrock-enabled regions. Regions are grouped by geographic area (NAMER, EMEA, APAC, LATAM), and each cell shows how a model is available in that region: on-demand (In Region), Cross-Region Inference (CRIS), or Mantle.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can filter the matrix by availability type to quickly answer questions like “which models support CRIS in Europe?” or “what is available on-demand in ap-southeast-2?” Expanding a model row reveals inference profile IDs, CRIS source regions, and geographic scopes. The matrix also surfaces model lifecycle status (active, legacy, or end-of-life), so you can identify models approaching deprecation and plan migrations in advance. For organizations operating across multiple geographies, this view replaces what would otherwise require dozens of individual API calls and manual cross-referencing of lifecycle documentation.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-20648-6.png" alt="Regional Availability matrix showing model availability per region, with color-coded cells indicating model status" width="600" height="824"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Regional Availability matrix showing model availability per region, with color-coded cells indicating model status&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="favorites"&gt;Favorites&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;As you explore models, you can star models to add them to your Favorites list, a personal shortlist that persists across browser sessions. The Favorites tab provides the same filtering, sorting, and detail views as the main explorer, scoped to just your saved models. This is useful for tracking a working set of candidate models during an evaluation, or for keeping quick access to the models your team uses most.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="use-cases"&gt;Use cases&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Let’s explore how different teams approach model selection based on their unique requirements. The following scenarios feature fictional organizations. They use real AWS pricing and quota data combined with realistic workload assumptions to demonstrate the solution’s capabilities.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="selecting-compliance-aligned-models-across-european-regions"&gt;Selecting compliance-aligned models across European regions&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Octank Financial Services needed to build an AI-powered document analysis system for regulatory compliance. The team required models with vision capabilities to process scanned documents, but operations had to remain exclusively within EU regions to meet data residency requirements. With dozens of multimodal models on Amazon Bedrock and pricing that varies across regions, the team estimated 6 to 8 hours of manual research just to shortlist candidates.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Using the Model Profiler, the team filtered by “vision” capability and EU regions to immediately see qualifying models. They used the Regional Availability matrix to confirm on-demand availability in eu-west-1 and eu-central-1, then compared pricing side by side to find the most cost-effective option. Service quotas confirmed that throughput would meet their projected 50,000 daily document reviews.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The result: model evaluation dropped from an estimated 8 hours to 25 minutes. The team also discovered that their preferred model was 18% cheaper in eu-west-1 than eu-central-1, and found a newer model with a larger context window that alleviated the need to split long regulatory documents across multiple calls.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="migrating-from-a-third-party-ai-provider"&gt;Migrating from a third-party AI provider&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Octank Health operated a clinical documentation assistant built on a third-party AI provider and needed to migrate to Amazon Bedrock for tighter AWS integration and cost control. Their requirements were specific: a context window of 128K tokens or more, low latency, and availability in at least three US regions for redundancy. Mapping their current provider’s capabilities to the Amazon Bedrock catalog manually across the different regions required extensive research across multiple documentation sources.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The team used capability filters to find models matching their requirements, then opened the comparison view to evaluate context windows, output limits, and pricing across candidates. The Regional Availability view confirmed which models supported cross-region inference with US scope, giving them automatic failover without application changes. Lifecycle status flags helped them avoid a model that was approaching legacy status.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The evaluation completed in under 45 minutes. The team selected a model with a 200K context window (a significant upgrade from their previous 128K limit) at 15% lower cost per token. The cross-region inference availability check helped prevent them from choosing a model without cross-region support, which would have required rearchitecting their failover strategy.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="multi-region-deployment-planning-global-expansion"&gt;Multi-region deployment: Planning global expansion&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Octank Media was expanding their AI-powered content recommendation engine from the US to Europe and Asia-Pacific. Before committing to an architecture, they needed to confirm their current model’s availability in target regions, understand which consumption options were supported in each, and verify that service quotas could handle projected traffic.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The Regional Availability matrix gave the team a single view of their model across all 33 regions, broken down by In Region, CRIS, and Mantle. Filtering by APAC and EMEA regions revealed exactly where their model was available and how. The lifecycle status column confirmed the model was active in the target regions with no upcoming deprecation. Quota comparison across regions identified ap-northeast-1 as a potential bottleneck requiring a quota increase request before launch.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The multi-region feasibility analysis completed in 20 minutes. Previously, the same work required querying individual regions through the AWS Command Line Interface (AWS CLI) and cross-referencing documentation. Identifying the quota constraint in Tokyo before production helped prevent an outage and confirming CRIS availability in Europe established a reliable fallback strategy for traffic spikes.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="prerequisites"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Before deploying the Model Profiler, confirm you have the following in place.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;For local deployment:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Python 3.11 or later.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Node.js 18 or later.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;AWS credentials configured with read access to the following services:&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;bedrock:ListFoundationModels, bedrock:ListInferenceProfiles
bedrock-mantle:*
pricing:GetProducts, pricing:DescribeServices
servicequotas:ListServiceQuotas, servicequotas:GetServiceQuota
ec2:DescribeRegions&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;For AWS deployment (additional requirements):&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;AWS Serverless Application Model (AWS SAM) CLI installed.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;AWS credentials with permissions to deploy CloudFormation stacks, Lambda functions, S3 buckets, Step Functions, and CloudFront distributions. The AWS SAM template creates the necessary IAM policies automatically, so you only need permission to deploy the stack itself.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="deployment-walkthrough"&gt;Deployment walkthrough&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The Model Profiler offers two deployment paths. Choose local deployment to explore quickly on your own machine, or the AWS serverless deployment for an automatically refreshed, hosted interface.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="option-1-local-deployment"&gt;Option 1: Local deployment&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Deploy the Model Profiler locally in under five minutes with no cloud infrastructure required.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="step-1-clone-the-repository"&gt;Step 1: Clone the repository&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;git clone https://github.com/aws-samples/bedrock-model-profiler.git
cd bedrock-model-profiler&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="step-2-set-up-the-python-environment"&gt;Step 2: Set up the Python environment&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;python -m venv .venv
source .venv/bin/activate # On Windows: .venv\Scripts\activate
pip install -r local/requirements.txt&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="step-3-collect-model-data"&gt;Step 3: Collect model data&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Run the data collector using your AWS profile. This queries APIs across 25 regions and typically completes in 1-2 minutes.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;python -m local collect --profile your-aws-profile&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You’ll see progress output as each phase completes:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;[1/8] Collecting pricing data...
Found 9562 pricing products
[2/8] Aggregating pricing data...
Aggregated into 18 providers
...
[8/8] Building final output...
Wrote data/bedrock_models.json
Wrote data/bedrock_pricing.json

Collection Complete!
Duration: 81.3 seconds
Models collected: 117&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="step-4-start-the-front-end"&gt;Step 4: Start the front end&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;cd frontend
npm install
cp template.env .env
npm run dev&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Navigate to &lt;a href="http://localhost:5173/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;http://localhost:5173&lt;/a&gt; in your browser. The application loads data directly from the local data/ directory.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="option-2-aws-deployment"&gt;Option 2: AWS deployment&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Deploy the full serverless solution with automated daily refresh.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="step-1-verify-your-aws-account"&gt;Step 1: Verify your AWS account&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws sts get-caller-identity&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If you have multiple profiles, set the one to use:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;export AWS_PROFILE=your-profile-name&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="step-2-deploy-the-backend-stack"&gt;Step 2: Deploy the backend stack&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The backend must be deployed first using AWS SAM. This creates the Lambda functions, Step Functions workflow, S3 data bucket, and EventBridge schedule.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;cd infra
sam build -t backend-template.yaml
sam deploy --guided&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The guided deployment prompts you for a stack name, region, and parameters. Use the defaults for a standard deployment or customize as needed.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4&gt;Step 3: Deploy the front end and link infrastructure&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Once the backend stack is running, the setup script deploys the remaining infrastructure and connects everything together.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;./setup-infrastructure.sh&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The script performs five steps:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Verifies the backend stack exists and retrieves the data bucket name.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Deploys the front-end infrastructure (S3 hosting bucket and CloudFront distribution).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Updates the backend stack with the CloudFront ARN so the data bucket grants read access to the CDN.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Optionally deploys the analytics stack if Amazon Cognito credentials are configured in frontend/.env.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Builds the React front end and uploads it to S3.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When complete, you’ll receive a CloudFront URL where the application is accessible. Data refreshes automatically every day at 6 AM UTC. For additional help, see the deployment guide in the repository at &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/bedrock-model-profiler#troubleshooting" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;https://github.com/aws-samples/bedrock-model-profiler&lt;/a&gt; or open an issue at &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/bedrock-model-profiler/issues" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;https://github.com/aws-samples/bedrock-model-profiler/issues&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="clean-up"&gt;Clean up&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If you deployed the AWS infrastructure for evaluation purposes, remove the resources once you’ve completed your model selection to avoid ongoing charges.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Leaving unused infrastructure deployed can accumulate unexpected costs over time, particularly if you’re running the profiler in multiple regions or accounts for testing. Additionally, removing evaluation stacks helps maintain a clean AWS environment and helps prevent confusion when managing production resources.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;For local deployment:&lt;/strong&gt; Delete the cloned repository directory. No cloud resources were created. &lt;strong&gt;For AWS deployment:&lt;/strong&gt; Delete the CloudFormation stack to remove all resources:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws cloudformation delete-stack --stack-name bedrock-profiler&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This removes the S3 buckets, Lambda functions, Step Functions workflow, and CloudFront distribution.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Selecting the right foundation model is an important decision when building generative AI applications, and it should not require days of manual research. The Amazon Bedrock Model Profiler consolidates capabilities, pricing, and regional availability for 120+ models into a single interface so your team can move from question to decision in minutes.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can deploy the Model Profiler locally in under five minutes with no cloud infrastructure required. If you prefer automated daily refreshes and a hosted interface, the serverless AWS deployment option takes just a few additional steps. Either way, you get the same complete view of the Amazon Bedrock catalog.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Ready to get started? Clone the repository at &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/bedrock-model-profiler" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;github.com/aws-samples/bedrock-model-profiler&lt;/a&gt; and deploy locally in under five minutes. Explore &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock&lt;/a&gt; and review &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/pricing/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock pricing&lt;/a&gt; to plan your model strategy. The project is open source under the MIT No Attribution (MIT-0) license. Bug reports, feature requests, and pull requests are welcome.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Related resources&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock User Guide&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/pricing/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock pricing&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/models/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock foundation models&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Important:&lt;/strong&gt; This repository contains sample code for educational or development purposes. Review and test thoroughly before production use. AWS services and model availability vary by Region and change over time. For current pricing information, see the &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/pricing/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock pricing page&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;

		<title>Accelerate protein design with BoltzGen on Amazon SageMaker AI</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/accelerate-protein-design-with-boltzgen-on-amazon-sagemaker-ai/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Hasun Yu, Ph.D.]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 01 Jul 2026 17:44:08 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Amazon SageMaker]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Simple Storage Service (S3)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Intermediate (200)]]></category>

		<guid isPermaLink="false">6eee4b18c553052c812e9898be4720a1b5f33aa8</guid>

					<description>In this post, we demonstrate how to deploy BoltzGen on SageMaker AI and run an end-to-end protein design experiment. By the end of the walkthrough, you have a working setup that scales from quick validation runs to production batch processing. The setup offers two execution modes for different stages of research and uses step-level caching to reduce compute expenses during iterative workflows.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://github.com/HannesStark/boltzgen" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;BoltzGen&lt;/a&gt; on &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/ai/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker AI&lt;/a&gt; accelerates protein binder design by managing GPU compute infrastructure end to end. &lt;a href="https://boltz.bio/boltzgen" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;BoltzGen&lt;/a&gt; is a diffusion-based generative model that designs proteins and peptides capable of binding to specific biomolecular targets. A typical design campaign involves multiple GPU-intensive steps: backbone generation, inverse folding, structural validation, and candidate ranking. Running these steps across hundreds, thousands, or even millions of design candidates introduces operational overhead in provisioning instances, moving data between steps, and tracking costs. SageMaker AI manages this compute lifecycle from instance provisioning through result delivery and resource cleanup, so you can focus on design iteration rather than infrastructure operations.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we demonstrate how to deploy BoltzGen on SageMaker AI and run an end-to-end protein design experiment. By the end of the walkthrough, you have a working setup that scales from quick validation runs to production batch processing. The setup offers two execution modes for different stages of research and uses step-level caching to reduce compute expenses during iterative workflows.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This walkthrough applies to academic research labs, biotech startups, pharmaceutical R&amp;amp;D groups, and educational programs, whether you work in protein binder design, therapeutic protein engineering, or de novo protein architecture.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="how-sagemaker-ai-addresses-protein-design-bottlenecks"&gt;How SageMaker AI addresses protein design bottlenecks&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Each step in a BoltzGen campaign runs on GPU hardware and processes one design specification at a time. On a 4-GPU instance (&lt;code&gt;ml.g5.12xlarge&lt;/code&gt;), a campaign of 1,000 samples takes approximately 375 hours to complete, based on the repository’s benchmark data. Operating this infrastructure involves building CUDA environments (e.g. install CUDA driver and setup toolkit), coordinating GPU instance lifecycles, constructing data pipelines between steps, and recovering from failures in long-running jobs.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;SageMaker AI addresses each of these bottlenecks directly. After you submit a job, SageMaker AI provisions GPU instances and executes BoltzGen inside the container. It writes results to &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/s3/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Simple Storage Service&lt;/a&gt; (Amazon S3) and releases the instances when processing completes. Billing is per-second, so there are no idle GPU costs. A 2-hour design run on &lt;code&gt;ml.g4dn.xlarge&lt;/code&gt; costs approximately $1.50 based on &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/pricing/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;on-demand pricing&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The implementation supports multi-GPU parallelization within a single instance and multi-instance scaling across a pipeline. In pipeline mode, each step’s output is cached in Amazon S3 with a 7-day expiry, so when you iterate on filtering parameters, the design generation step that accounts for approximately 90 percent of compute cost does not re-run.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Setup scripts in the repository build the container and push it to &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/ecr/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Elastic Container Registry&lt;/a&gt; (Amazon ECR), and working examples let you submit your first design job within minutes. The same configuration that validates a design specification with 10 candidates scales to larger campaigns — only the parameter values change. Instance types ranging from &lt;code&gt;ml.g4dn&lt;/code&gt; (lowest cost T4 GPUs) to &lt;code&gt;ml.g6e&lt;/code&gt; (NVIDIA L40S GPUs) give you flexibility for matching throughput to budget.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="what-boltzgen-offers"&gt;What BoltzGen offers&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;BoltzGen is an all-atom generative model for designing proteins and peptides that can bind to a wide range of biomolecular targets. The model generates binder backbone structures through a diffusion process. It then uses an inverse folding model called BoltzIF to generate amino acid sequences and validates structures with &lt;a href="https://github.com/jwohlwend/boltz" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Boltz2 folding predictions&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The implementation is available in the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-biofm-quickstart/tree/main/models/boltzgen/inference/amazon-sagemaker/boltzgen-on-sagemaker-processing-job-cli" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Boltzgen on SageMaker repository on GitHub&lt;/a&gt;. The repository includes setup scripts, both execution modes, multi-GPU and multi-instance scaling support, and a quick start guide.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="two-execution-modes"&gt;Two execution modes&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We provided two execution modes in our implementation, optimized for different stages of research, so you start with quick experiments and graduate to production workflows as needs grow.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/processing-job.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;SageMaker AI processing jobs&lt;/a&gt; provide direct batch execution for quick experiments. After submitting a job, SageMaker AI provisions the GPU instance, runs the container, and shuts down the instance when complete. A single Python script triggers the entire workflow, without multi-step orchestration. For early-stage work where the goal is to test design specifications before scaling up, processing jobs minimize setup time.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/pipelines-overview.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;SageMaker AI Pipelines&lt;/a&gt; provide a 5-step orchestrated workflow with step-level caching, automatic scaling, and visual monitoring in SageMaker AI Studio. Production workloads benefit from parameter iteration without re-running expensive compute steps. Each step (design, inverse folding, folding, analysis, filtering) can be scaled independently, and cached results are reused when inputs have not changed.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Both modes follow the same workflow. The container from Amazon ECR runs on a provisioned GPU instance, BoltzGen processes the design specification, and SageMaker AI stores results in Amazon S3 before shutting down compute resources.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="getting-started"&gt;Getting started&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To get started, you need an AWS account, a few tools installed locally, and an Amazon S3 bucket for your data. The following sections walk you through each prerequisite and then guide you through your first protein design experiment.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="prerequisites"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Make sure you have an active AWS account with billing enabled and the &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/cli/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Command Line Interface&lt;/a&gt; (AWS CLI) installed and configured (&lt;code&gt;aws configure&lt;/code&gt;). Verify that you have sufficient service quotas (for example, 1 for this walkthrough) for GPU instances (&lt;code&gt;ml.g4dn.xlarge&lt;/code&gt; or higher) in your target AWS Region (such as &lt;code&gt;us-east-1&lt;/code&gt;).&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Next, create an &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/iam/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Identity and Access Management&lt;/a&gt; (IAM) execution role for SageMaker AI. Attach the &lt;code&gt;AmazonSageMakerFullAccess&lt;/code&gt; managed policy, grant Amazon S3 read/write access to your data bucket, and grant Amazon ECR pull permissions for the BoltzGen container image.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;On your local machine, install Python 3.11+ with pip, the latest Boto3 and SageMaker SDK (&lt;code&gt;pip install boto3 sagemaker&lt;/code&gt;), and Docker for building container images. Finally, create an Amazon S3 bucket in your target Region:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws s3 mb s3://amzn-s3-demo-bucket --region &amp;lt;aws-region&amp;gt;&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="running-your-first-protein-design"&gt;Running your first protein design&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For the most streamlined setup experience, we recommend &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/ai/studio/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker Studio&lt;/a&gt; or an &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/nbi.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker notebook&lt;/a&gt; instance. These environments come preconfigured with AWS credentials, the SageMaker SDK, and Docker support.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="launch-sagemaker-ai-studio"&gt;Launch SageMaker AI Studio&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Start by opening SageMaker AI Studio, where you run the setup and experiment commands.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Open the &lt;a href="https://console.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;SageMaker AI console&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Choose &lt;strong&gt;Studio&lt;/strong&gt; from the left navigation.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Create a new Studio domain (if one does not exist) or open an existing one, &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/studio-updated-local-get-started.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;enable Docker Access through local mode&lt;/a&gt; using AWS CLI.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Launch a JupyterLab space with an instance (for example, &lt;code&gt;ml.m5.2xlarge&lt;/code&gt;).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Raise quota limits for &lt;code&gt;ml.g4dn.xlarge&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;ml.g5.xlarge&lt;/code&gt; processing job usage to greater than 0.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="clone-the-repository"&gt;Clone the repository&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Download the BoltzGen on SageMaker AI code, which includes setup scripts, both execution modes, and example design specifications.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;git clone https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-biofm-quickstart.git
cd sample-biofm-quickstart/models/boltzgen/inference/amazon-sagemaker/boltzgen-on-sagemaker-processing-job-cli&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="build-and-push-the-container-image"&gt;Build and push the container image&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Your container image packages BoltzGen with its dependencies and GPU drivers. Build it once and reuse it across experiments. Set your region and run the build script:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;export AWS_REGION=&amp;lt;aws-region&amp;gt;
./sagemaker/build_and_push.sh&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="configure-aws-credentials"&gt;Configure AWS credentials&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;SageMaker AI needs your AWS account details to provision compute resources and store results in Amazon S3. Copy the environment template and fill in your settings:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;cp sagemaker/pipeline/.env.example sagemaker/pipeline/.env
vim sagemaker/pipeline/.env&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Example &lt;code&gt;.env&lt;/code&gt;:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;AWS_REGION=us-east-1
AWS_S3_BUCKET=amzn-s3-demo-bucket
AWS_ROLE_ARN=arn:aws:iam::&amp;lt;aws-account-id&amp;gt;:role/SageMakerExecutionRole
AWS_IMAGE_URI=&amp;lt;aws-account-id&amp;gt;.dkr.ecr.&amp;lt;aws-region&amp;gt;.amazonaws.com/boltzgen-sagemaker:latest&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Now you’re ready to run your first design job with two options:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Option A:&lt;/strong&gt; Quick experiment with all steps in a single processing job:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;python sagemaker/run_processing_job.py \
  --design-spec example/vanilla_protein/1g13prot.yaml \
  --s3-bucket amzn-s3-demo-bucket \
  --instance-type ml.g4dn.xlarge \
  --num-designs 10 \
  --budget 2 \
  --wait&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You have 10 intermediate candidate designs to generate using the diffusion model, which are the raw backbone structures going through inverse folding, refolding, analysis and filtering. In practice, this number should be much higher like 10,000 to 60,000 for real runs. The final diversity-optimized output set has two designs. So out of the 10 intermediate designs, only the two optimized for both quality and sequence diversity end up in your output folder.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Option B:&lt;/strong&gt; A more production-like workflow that orchestrates different steps in a pipeline.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Change to pipeline directory:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;cd sagemaker/pipeline&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Create the pipeline:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;python run_pipeline.py --config pipeline_config.yaml create&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Run with design specifications:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;python run_pipeline.py --config pipeline_config.yaml run&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Check status (replace the Amazon Resource Name (ARN) with the execution ARN):&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;python run_pipeline.py --region us-east-1 status --execution-arn &amp;lt;ARN&amp;gt;&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="download-results"&gt;Download results&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For processing job:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws s3 sync s3://amzn-s3-demo-bucket/boltzgen/output/boltzgen-TIMESTAMP ./results&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For pipeline:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws s3 sync s3://amzn-s3-demo-bucket/boltzgen-pipeline/output/TIMESTAMP ./results&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The output directory contains several subdirectories. &lt;code&gt;final_ranked_designs/&lt;/code&gt; holds the top designs ranked by predicted binding affinity. &lt;code&gt;intermediate_designs/&lt;/code&gt; contains the generated candidate structures. &lt;code&gt;metrics/&lt;/code&gt; stores structural analysis data including root-mean-square deviation (RMSD) and contact scores. A &lt;code&gt;job_metadata.json&lt;/code&gt; file records the complete job parameters and status.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following directory tree shows the output structure from a run with &lt;code&gt;--num-designs 100 --budget 10&lt;/code&gt; against the 1G13 target:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;results/1g13prot/
├── final_ranked_designs/
│   ├── final_10_designs/                   # Top designs selected for diversity and quality (.cif)
│   ├── intermediate_ranked_10_designs/     # Top designs ranked by quality score (.cif)
│   ├── all_designs_metrics.csv             # Full metrics table for all designs
│   ├── final_designs_metrics_10.csv        # Metrics for final selected designs
│   └── results_overview.pdf                # Summary report with filtering criteria and plots
├── intermediate_designs/                   # All generated backbone structures (.cif)
├── intermediate_designs_inverse_folded/    # Structures after sequence design and refolding
└── config/                                 # Step configurations used for reproducibility&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;BoltzGen automatically filters and ranks all generated designs. Each design must pass structural quality thresholds—including refolding RMSD below 2.5 Angstrom and balanced amino acid composition—before entering the ranking stage. In this run, 3 out of 100 designs passed all filters. The pipeline then ranks designs by a composite quality score and applies diversity optimization to select the final set.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following table shows the top five designs with key metrics. Higher &lt;code&gt;design_ptm&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;design_iptm&lt;/code&gt; scores indicate greater structural confidence and binding strength. Lower &lt;code&gt;filter_rmsd&lt;/code&gt; indicates better structural accuracy after refolding, and higher &lt;code&gt;delta_sasa&lt;/code&gt; indicates a larger binding interface.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt;

&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Each final design is saved as a &lt;code&gt;.cif&lt;/code&gt; structure file with a rank prefix (for example, &lt;code&gt;rank001_1g13prot_24.cif&lt;/code&gt;), ready for downstream analysis or experimental validation.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Now that your first experiment is complete, the following sections explore how BoltzGen on SageMaker AI works under the hood so you can optimize the architecture for your specific needs.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="how-it-works"&gt;How it works&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Understanding the architecture and scaling capabilities behind both execution modes helps you choose the right instance type and decide between single-instance and multi-instance configurations. It also helps you take full advantage of caching to reduce costs.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="architecture"&gt;Architecture&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;With processing-job mode (single-step batch), SageMaker AI coordinates instance provisioning, container execution, Amazon S3 data movement, and cleanup after you submit your job. Your workflow follows these steps:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/17/ML-20375-1.png" alt="Architecture diagram for processing job mode showing SageMaker AI provisioning a GPU instance, executing the BoltzGen container against Amazon S3 input data, and writing results back to Amazon S3" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Prepare a design specification (YAML) and reference structure files.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Upload your files to Amazon S3. SageMaker AI mounts the Amazon S3 input data to your container.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;BoltzGen generates designs and writes results back to Amazon S3.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Download your final results from Amazon S3.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In pipeline mode (5-step workflow), your pipeline runs five sequential steps, each of which you can scale separately:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/17/ML-20375-2.png" alt="Pipeline mode architecture diagram showing five sequential steps: Design, Inverse Folding, Folding, Analysis, and Filtering, with cached intermediate outputs in Amazon S3" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Design (GPU): Diffusion model generates binder backbone structures.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Inverse folding (GPU): BoltzIF generates amino acid sequences.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Folding (GPU): Boltz2 validates complex structures.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Analysis (CPU): Calculates structural metrics (RMSD, solvent-accessible surface area (SASA), and contacts).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Filtering (CPU): Ranks and selects top designs.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Your pipeline caches each step’s output in Amazon S3 with 7-day expiry. When inputs haven’t changed, cached results are reused automatically, so when you iterate on filtering parameters, the expensive design generation step is skipped entirely.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="scaling-across-gpus-and-instances"&gt;Scaling across GPUs and instances&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;BoltzGen on SageMaker AI supports two complementary scaling strategies that help you balance cost and throughput.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Multi-GPU parallelization distributes design specifications across GPUs within a single instance through round-robin scheduling. Each GPU processes its assigned specifications independently using &lt;code&gt;ProcessPoolExecutor&lt;/code&gt; (Python’s built-in parallel processing library), with thread-safe progress tracking through a &lt;code&gt;ProgressTracker&lt;/code&gt; class. GPU detection is automatic through &lt;code&gt;nvidia-smi&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For example, on an &lt;code&gt;ml.g5.12xlarge&lt;/code&gt; instance with 4 GPUs, 10 design specifications are distributed as follows:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;10 design specs -&amp;gt; GPU0: specs 0,4,8 | GPU1: specs 1,5,9 | GPU2: specs 2,6 | GPU3: specs 3,7&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A single multi-GPU instance is more efficient than multiple single-GPU instances because BoltzGen loads approximately 5 GB of model weights. One multi-GPU instance avoids redundant model loading, reducing startup time and memory overhead.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Multi-instance scaling extends parallelism beyond a single machine. In pipeline mode, each step supports multi-instance execution. Work is partitioned automatically. For example, instance 0 processes the first half of specifications, and instance 1 processes the second half. Each instance then further parallelizes across its own GPUs.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;def get_worker_info() -&amp;gt; Tuple[int, int]:
    """Auto-detect worker ID from SageMaker environment."""
    hosts_str = os.environ.get("SM_HOSTS", "")
    current_host = os.environ.get("SM_CURRENT_HOST", "")

    if hosts_str and current_host:
        hosts = json.loads(hosts_str)
        instance_count = len(hosts)
        worker_id = hosts.index(current_host)
        return worker_id, instance_count

    return 0, 1  # Default single instance&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="step-level-caching"&gt;Step-level caching&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Pipeline mode caches each step’s output based on input hashes with a 7-day expiry. Because the design step accounts for approximately 90 percent of compute cost, caching provides substantial savings during iterative work. Changing only the filtering parameters triggers a rerun of only the filtering step, while cached design outputs are preserved.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;# pipeline.py configuration
cache_config=CacheConfig(
    enable_caching=True,
    expire_after="7d"  # 7-day expiry
)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="container-configuration-and-implementation"&gt;Container, configuration, and implementation&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Your Docker image (&lt;code&gt;Dockerfile.sagemaker&lt;/code&gt;) is built on NVIDIA CUDA 12.2 with cuDNN 8. It includes Python 3.10, build tools (&lt;code&gt;cmake&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;gcc&lt;/code&gt;), scientific libraries (HDF5, Boost), and PyTorch with CUDA 12.1 support. SageMaker AI maps standard mount paths: &lt;code&gt;/opt/ml/processing/input&lt;/code&gt; for input data, &lt;code&gt;/opt/ml/processing/output&lt;/code&gt; for output data, and &lt;code&gt;/opt/ml/processing/cache&lt;/code&gt; for model weights.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Configure your pipeline through YAML files with environment variable substitution:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-yaml"&gt;aws:
  region: ${AWS_REGION}           # From .env file
  s3_bucket: ${AWS_S3_BUCKET}
  role_arn: ${AWS_ROLE_ARN}
  image_uri: ${AWS_IMAGE_URI}

pipeline:
  name: BoltzGen-Protein-Design
  s3_prefix: boltzgen-pipeline

design:
  num_designs: 10000
  budget: 100
  protocol: protein-anything
  specs_dir: ./specs

instances:
  design:
    type: ml.g5.12xlarge   # 4 GPUs for parallel processing
    instance_count: 1      # Increase for multi-instance scaling&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The syntax supports &lt;code&gt;${VAR_NAME}&lt;/code&gt; for variables loaded from &lt;code&gt;.env&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;${VAR_NAME:default}&lt;/code&gt; for variables with default values.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The core integration relies on the SageMaker AI &lt;code&gt;ScriptProcessor&lt;/code&gt; class:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;processor = ScriptProcessor(
    role=role,                           # IAM role with Amazon S3 + Amazon ECR access
    image_uri=&amp;lt;image_uri&amp;gt;,               # Amazon ECR image URI
    instance_count=1,                    # Single GPU instance
    instance_type='ml.g4dn.xlarge',      # NVIDIA T4 GPU
    volume_size_in_gb=50,                # EBS storage for models
    max_runtime_in_seconds=86400,        # 24-hour timeout
    command=['python3']                  # Execute Python scripts
)

processor.run(
    code='processing_script.py',
    inputs=[ProcessingInput(source=s3_input, destination='/opt/ml/processing/input')],
    outputs=[ProcessingOutput(source='/opt/ml/processing/output', destination=s3_output)],
    arguments=['--design-spec', 'design.yaml', '--num-designs', '100', '--budget', '10']
)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Intermediate results between pipeline steps are stored in Amazon S3:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;Amazon S3 Intermediate Storage:
+-- designs/              &amp;lt;- Design step output
|   +-- spec_name/
|       +-- intermediate_designs/*.cif
+-- inverse_folded/       &amp;lt;- Inverse folding output
+-- folded/               &amp;lt;- Folding validation output
+-- analyzed/             &amp;lt;- Analysis metrics output
+-- metadata/             &amp;lt;- Step execution metadata&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;On your first BoltzGen run, approximately 6 GB of model weights are downloaded from Hugging Face. The cache directory (&lt;code&gt;/opt/ml/processing/cache&lt;/code&gt;) stores these weights, and subsequent runs in the same container reuse the cached models.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="cost-optimization-tips"&gt;Cost optimization tips&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Start small. We recommend testing with &lt;code&gt;--num-designs 10 --budget 2&lt;/code&gt; first to verify that your design specification works correctly before scaling up.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Match the mode to your stage. Processing jobs work well for quick experiments and validation. Pipelines suit production runs where parameter iteration benefits from step-level caching.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We recommend that you use caching. In pipeline mode, model weights (approximately 6 GB) are downloaded only on the first run, and step-level caching skips completed compute steps when inputs haven’t changed. Together, these two layers of caching save substantial time and cost during iterative workflows.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="clean-up"&gt;Clean up&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To avoid ongoing charges after completing your experiments, delete the resources you created during this walkthrough.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Delete Amazon S3 data:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws s3 rm s3://amzn-s3-demo-bucket/boltzgen --recursive&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Delete Amazon ECR repository:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws ecr delete-repository --repository-name boltzgen-sagemaker --force&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Delete Amazon S3 bucket (if no longer needed):&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws s3 rb s3://amzn-s3-demo-bucket&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Delete AWS IAM role (optional):&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws iam detach-role-policy --role-name BoltzGenSageMakerRole --policy-arn arn:aws:iam::aws:policy/AmazonSageMakerFullAccess
aws iam delete-role --role-name BoltzGenSageMakerRole&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Stop and delete the JupyterLab space if you followed the preceding instructions. SageMaker AI Studio spaces generate cost because they run on a dedicated instance, in this case an &lt;code&gt;ml.m5.2xlarge&lt;/code&gt; instance. Stop the space from the Studio UI or the SageMaker AI console. You can also stop it by using the AWS CLI. See &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/studio-updated-running-stop.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;the instructions in the SageMaker AI documentation&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we demonstrated how to deploy BoltzGen on SageMaker AI, run your first protein design experiment, and scale from quick validation runs to production batch processing. Managed GPU compute, step-level caching, and per-second billing make it possible to move from a design specification to ranked protein candidates without managing infrastructure. Two execution modes let you match compute to each stage of research: processing jobs for quick experiments and pipelines for production workflows. Multi-GPU parallelization takes a single validation run to thousands of designs.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="next-steps"&gt;Next steps&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To get started, visit the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-biofm-quickstart/tree/main"&gt;sample-biofm-quickstart&lt;/a&gt; repository on GitHub and follow the quick start guide for &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-biofm-quickstart/tree/main/models/boltzgen/inference/amazon-sagemaker/boltzgen-on-sagemaker-processing-job-cli"&gt;Boltzgen on SageMaker processing job&lt;/a&gt;. For more information about SageMaker AI processing jobs and pipelines, see the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/"&gt;Amazon SageMaker AI documentation&lt;/a&gt;. We welcome feedback and contributions to the project. For questions about scaling protein design needs using SageMaker AI, reach out to an AWS representative.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="acknowledgments"&gt;Acknowledgments&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We thank the &lt;a href="https://boltz.bio/manifesto" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;BoltzGen team&lt;/a&gt; for developing this protein design model.&lt;/p&gt;

		<item>
		<title>Safely Releasing Frontier Models to Customers</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/safely-releasing-frontier-models-to-customers/</link>
					
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Amy Herzog]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 01 Jul 2026 03:13:19 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Amazon Bedrock]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Machine Learning]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Announcements]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Featured]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Generative AI]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Launch]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[News]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Security & Governance]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Thought Leadership]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">04ab21c59727c995ed236adb9de722150c09753b</guid>

					<description>It’s our goal for AWS to be the most secure place to run any workload, and in support of that we’ve been deeply investing in security across our services since&amp;nbsp;AWS's&amp;nbsp;inception more than two decades ago. Our AI services like Amazon Bedrock are built on this foundation and with the same focus. </description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;It’s our goal for AWS to be the most secure place to run any workload, and in support of that we’ve been deeply investing in security across our services since&amp;nbsp;AWS’s&amp;nbsp;inception more than two decades ago.  Our AI services like Amazon Bedrock are built on this foundation and with the same focus. Bedrock provides customers with world-class&amp;nbsp;performance,&amp;nbsp;security and privacy as well as the broadest selection of models available anywhere.  Last year we launched Bedrock Mantle with&amp;nbsp;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/exploring-the-zero-operator-access-design-of-mantle/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;industry-leading privacy&lt;/a&gt;  and protection for model weights. We regularly hear from customers that they want access to the latest models as quickly as possible after they’re released and Bedrock delivers this for them along with the enterprise features that customers expect from AWS. We are excited that Anthropic’s Claude Fable 5 models are available again to our customers on Bedrock, and that they feature even stronger guardrails to prevent misuse.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When releasing models, we consider not only our responsibilities to our customers, but to the Internet and society as a whole. The most recent generation of frontier models, such as Anthropic’s Claude Mythos have powerful new capabilities, particularly in the area of cybersecurity. We’ve been able to experience this first hand as part of Project Glasswing and we’re eager to get Mythos-class models into the hands of defenders.  As defenders, we have the opportunity to use these models to make the systems on which we all depend materially more secure. But as we do, we must also ensure that we don’t also give our adversaries meaningfully advanced visibility and capabilities, without giving companies, governments, and academic institutions the opportunity to protect their assets first. Striking this balance is a key challenge for broad model release, which is why we’ve been working closely with Anthropic and other industry partners in Project Glasswing to refine the guardrails for this new class of models. We all agree that preventing adversaries from gaining access to the ability to do deep vulnerability research is the most important objective for these guardrails.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This is also an exciting time for AI, with new capabilities being delivered almost daily.  We believe that making the capabilities of these advanced models available to all customers in a secure, privacy preserving environment is critical to ensuring that they can get&amp;nbsp;the&amp;nbsp;many benefits without creating security risks.&amp;nbsp;It’s important that new guardrails continue to be developed as we learn more about how well the current ones are working and as new models get released. We’re going to keep iterating with our partners, delivering more value, and being responsive to changes in the industry.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;It’s equally important to make sure any issues with these models after they are released get addressed appropriately. Anthropic has published a blog, &lt;a href="https://www.anthropic.com/news/redeploying-fable-5" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Redeploying Fable 5&lt;/a&gt;, that explains how they’re thinking about the capabilities of this new class of models, and their commitments and SLAs for responding to issues that are reported to them.  We appreciate Anthropic’s transparency and collaboration in articulating this first structure for issue severity and response for cyber-capable models, and we look forward to the ongoing conversation across our industry as we learn and refine it.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Our AI Red Team has worked with Anthropic to further improve Fable’s protections, and we believe its latest guardrails result in a very capable model that further minimizes the risk of misuse by adversaries. It delivers on the promise of much stronger reasoning capabilities in most domains, without giving adversaries significant new security capabilities. When the guardrails are triggered, it automatically falls back to Opus 4.8, itself a world-class model that is already publicly accessible.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We appreciate Anthropic’s partnership and commitment to defenders, and look forward to working with them and the rest of the industry to continue to make frontier models available safely and securely.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;hr&gt;

Prior to joining AWS, Amy served as CISO for Amazon’s Devices and Services, Media and Entertainment, and Advertising businesses, overseeing the security of consumer technology offerings such as Alexa+ and Ring, and playing a key role in the secure development of Project Kuiper, Amazon’s initiative to provide fast, reliable broadband to customers and communities around the world through low earth orbit satellites.&lt;/p&gt;
