   &lt;td&gt;5&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;0&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;0&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Multi-tenant isolation demo output&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="cleanup"&gt;Cleanup&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Warning: The following cleanup steps will permanently delete CloudWatch logs. If you need to retain logs for audit or analysis purposes, export them before proceeding with cleanup.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To avoid ongoing charges in your account, &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-resilient-llm-inference?tab=readme-ov-file#cleanup" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;complete these steps&lt;/a&gt; to stop the gateway and delete the CloudWatch logs.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="important-considerations"&gt;Important considerations&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The preceding guidance makes certain assumptions about workload requirements, data security and compliance obligations, and performance needs. As with most things, the answer to “When should I use these patterns?” is “It depends!”. The following are a few use cases where applying these patterns makes sense.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="applicable-scenarios-and-use-cases"&gt;Applicable scenarios and use cases&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;High availability requirements&lt;/strong&gt;: When your application cannot tolerate downtime, multiple models provide automatic failover. If your primary model hits rate limits or experiences outages, requests route to backup models, maintaining 100% availability.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Scaling beyond single model quotas&lt;/strong&gt;: Individual models have throughput limits. Using multiple models (even the same model type across different accounts/Regions) multiplies your total available capacity. This is essential for high-volume applications where request volume can exceed single model quotas.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Multi-tenant isolation&lt;/strong&gt;: In software as a service (SaaS) applications, different customers can use different models or model instances, helping prevent one customer’s usage from impacting others.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Development vs production&lt;/strong&gt;: Separate model configurations for testing (cheaper, faster models) and production (higher quality models) without code changes.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This post covered multiple patterns for resilient LLM inference, starting with Amazon Bedrock native cross-Region inference and ending with more complex patterns that required implementing an LLM gateway. Using these strategies, you can improve the resilience of your generative AI powered workloads and gain granular control over model availability and failover strategies, including dedicated control for specific consumers and applications.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We invite you to experiment with these patterns in detail using the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-resilient-llm-inference" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;accompanying GitHub repository&lt;/a&gt;, which provides code samples and step-by-step instructions for testing each pattern. For a comprehensive reference implementation of a production-ready generative LLM gateway, explore the &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/solutions/guidance/multi-provider-generative-ai-gateway-on-aws/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Solution for Multi-Provider Generative AI Gateway&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For more generative AI architectural patterns and best practices, visit the &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Artificial Intelligence Blog&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If you have any comments or questions, leave them in the comments section.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;TAGS: Generative AI, Amazon Bedrock, Resilience, Architecture&lt;/p&gt;

			</item>
		<item>
		<title>How Outpost VFX Uses AWS to Accelerate AI Model Training for Visual Effects</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/how-outpost-vfx-uses-aws-to-accelerate-ai-model-training-for-visual-effects/</link>
					
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Alex Newton]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 30 Jun 2026 16:37:09 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Amazon EC2]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon SageMaker AI]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Content Production]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Customer Solutions]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Europe]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Media & Entertainment]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">77505a7d40abc62ddfe122cbd077c28faf5ebad2</guid>

					<description>In this post, we explore how Outpost VFX achieved 8x faster training speeds using AWS infrastructure to transform their face replacement workflow, the technical architecture they implemented to overcome single-GPU limitations, and the measurable results achieved through AWS multi-GPU training.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;This post was co-written with Tim Chauncey and Dheeraj Bhadani of Outpost VFX.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;AI model training for visual effects (VFX) can take weeks, creating bottlenecks in production timelines. For Outpost VFX, which operates studios across the UK, Canada, and India delivering high-end film and episodic content, every day of delay impacts client deliverables and project schedules.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we explore how Outpost VFX achieved 8x faster training speeds using AWS infrastructure to transform their face replacement workflow, the technical architecture they implemented to overcome single-GPU limitations, and the measurable results achieved through AWS multi-GPU training.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2&gt;The challenge: Single-GPU bottlenecks in AI training&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Traditional face replacement workflows in visual effects production require over 5 days of compositing or specialist beauty and de-aging support to create initial versions for director approval. While effective, these methods create bottlenecks early in the iterative approval process, the phase that is most critical to production timelines. For VFX professionals, slow AI training translates directly to missed deadlines, increased costs, and delayed client feedback cycles.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Outpost VFX had developed an AI model capable of training on on-set footage to accelerate face replacement processes. However, efficiency was constrained by single-GPU compute limitations. The existing face swap tool could only utilize one GPU at a time, limiting video random access memory (VRAM) access and processing capacity for model training operations. This prevented the team from realizing the full potential of their AI-assisted approach.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2&gt;Design considerations&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Outpost VFX identified three critical technical requirements for optimizing their AI workflow:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Compute scalability&lt;/strong&gt; – The team needed to parallelize face replacement model training across multiple GPUs to achieve meaningful efficiency improvements. Single-GPU training was creating week-long delays in model iteration cycles.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Infrastructure security&lt;/strong&gt; – As an AWS customer since 2022 with a fully virtualized technology stack, Outpost VFX needed the solution to adhere to its exacting security requirements for processing highly sensitive production data.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Performance optimization&lt;/strong&gt; – Beyond raw speed improvements, the architecture needed to support larger datasets and higher-resolution images to improve output quality.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To address these requirements, Outpost VFX collaborated with AWS Generative AI Innovation Center developers who worked as an extension of their technology department to modernize their AI learning algorithms. The AWS Generative AI Innovation Center is a team of strategists, data scientists, engineers, and solutions architects that works step-by-step with customers to build bespoke solutions that harness the power of generative AI. Learn more about how to engage with the team on the &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/ai/generative-ai/innovation-center/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"&gt;Generative AI Innovation Center&lt;/a&gt; webpage.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2&gt;Architecture implementation&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The solution involved adapting the Outpost VFX existing face swap model codebase to support distributed GPU training across multiple GPUs. The implementation used AWS multi-GPU Amazon Elastic Compute Cloud (Amazon EC2) P5 instances within a segregated, secure cloud environment that aligned with the Outpost VFX existing infrastructure requirements.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Originally, Outpost VFX trained their face swap models on GPU-accelerated workstations. This involved collecting small datasets of actors and their stunt doubles and fine-tuning a base model on RTX 3090 GPUs. While this method worked, the Outpost team found that training time was slow, at around 1–2 weeks per fine-tune. Scaling up would have been difficult because of the management overhead of those cloud workstations. At this point, they looked at training on P5 instances.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;P5 instances feature NVIDIA H100 GPUs, which are purpose-built for distributed training workloads. Unlike G-series instances that use PCIe communication between GPUs, P5 instances provide NV Link interconnects offering significantly higher bandwidth for gradient synchronization, which is a critical factor when training across multiple GPUs. The H100’s 14,592 CUDA cores and 80GB of high-bandwidth HBM3 memory also represented a substantial upgrade over their local RTX 3090 setup.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Outpost VFX worked with the Generative AI Innovation Center to help them get their model running on the P5 instances. Over a 6-week advisory period, AWS scientists converted the model code to use PyTorch Distributed Data Parallel (DDP) training strategy. DDP is a parallelization technique that copies model weights to each GPU, allowing the system to process more images in each training batch. This approach increases the number of images that can be fitted into each batch, directly accelerating the training process.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The technical implementation included multi-GPU parallelization of face replacement model training, enhanced security architecture for sensitive production data, and integration with Outpost VFX existing AWS-based technology stack. As Outpost VFX continues to evolve their AI pipeline, the team sees potential in services like Amazon SageMaker AI with managed training, model versioning and hosted inference to further streamline how they develop and deploy models across their global studios.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img loading="lazy" class="size-full wp-image-132389 alignnone" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/05/26/image-ML-20295-1.png" alt="" width="1107" height="431"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2&gt;Measuring performance improvements&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To test the speed improvement of the multi-GPU training, Outpost VFX collected an image dataset for training, fixed model hyperparameters, and measured the time for the training to reach a specific loss threshold. They set the baseline as one GPU on a G5 instance compared to running the models on the P5 instances.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The combined development effort between Outpost VFX and AWS achieved up to 8x improvement in face replacement model learning speeds. This performance increase directly translated to faster iteration cycles, enabling more rapid director approval processes for early versions. The ability to train models on higher-resolution images and larger datasets improved output quality. Most significantly, v001 delivery to clients for initial review now takes 2 days, compared to the previous 1–2 week timeline.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;blockquote&gt;
 &lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;“We are now able to iterate much faster thanks to our parallelized workflow and the ability to harness multiple top-end GPUs at once,” &lt;/em&gt;explains Tim Chauncey, CTO of Outpost VFX.&lt;em&gt; “Speed of iteration is critical to VFX work, and this architecture provides more robust and scalable capabilities for future development.”&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/blockquote&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A future improvement could include increasing the quality of image outputs. Outpost could increase the image resolutions passed to the model and use newer generations of Amazon EC2 P5 instances with more VRAM to process these larger images and larger datasets.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The AWS-optimized architecture enables Outpost VFX to offer enhanced AI-assisted face replacement capabilities to clients while maintaining the security and scalability requirements of high-end visual effects production. The parallelized workflow architecture including a migration from local consumer NVIDIA GPUs to enterprise NVIDIA GPUs provides a foundation for future AI tool development and scaling across Outpost VFX global studio operations.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;blockquote&gt;
 &lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;“What excites me most is that these models are no longer research experiments; they are becoming an integral part of the modern VFX pipeline,” &lt;/em&gt;says Dheeraj Bhadani, Lead Software Architect at Outpost VFX.&lt;em&gt; “Multi-GPU acceleration is the foundation on which next-generation creative tools will be built.”&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/blockquote&gt; 
&lt;h2&gt;Next steps&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If you’re looking to accelerate your own AI training workflows, consider these steps:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Evaluate your current GPU utilization: Identify whether single-GPU constraints are limiting your training performance&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Explore multi-GPU architectures: Amazon EC2 P5 instances provide scalable compute for distributed training workloads&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Engage with AWS Generative AI Innovation Center: the same team that helped Outpost VFX parallelize their training workflow&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can achieve similar results by implementing distributed training strategies tailored to your specific use case and infrastructure requirements.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3&gt;Acknowledgments&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The authors would like to thank the following contributors for their support on this project Josh Chappatte, Laksh Puri and Ruchi Bhatia.&lt;/p&gt;

  &lt;div class="blog-author-image"&gt;
   &lt;img loading="lazy" class="alignleft wp-image-132396" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/05/26/image-ML-20295-8.png" alt="Tim Chauncey" width="100" height="125"&gt;
  &lt;/div&gt; 
  &lt;div style="overflow: hidden"&gt;

He is now leading a team integrating bleeding-edge ML production tools and agentic systems into Outpost’s production workflows.&lt;/p&gt;

		<item>
		<title>Building bilingual NER for cargo logistics with Amazon Bedrock</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/building-bilingual-ner-for-cargo-logistics-with-amazon-bedrock/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Manu Raj L S]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 30 Jun 2026 16:33:46 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Amazon Bedrock]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Customer Solutions]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">4bcdad0ccd08552680d001ae7d895bf42e27b109</guid>

					<description>In this post, we share the technical approach using token-based distillation, lessons learned, and deployment architecture. If you face similar bilingual NER challenges, you can benefit from IBS Software’s experience with the Amazon Bedrock knowledge distillation capabilities.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://www.ibsplc.com/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;IBS Software&lt;/a&gt;’s Cargo system processes thousands of bilingual cargo logistics email messages daily. The system extracts critical information such as air waybill (AWB) numbers, flight details, weights, and delivery instructions in both English and Japanese. This added to the complexity of building a robust Named Entity Recognition (NER) solution. Challenges included manual intervention that slowed operations and a trade-off between accuracy and cost. IBS Software needed an AI solution that could accurately identify 23 different entity types across two languages while remaining cost-effective at scale.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After exploring multiple approaches, IBS Software used managed distillation capabilities of Amazon Bedrock to create a production-ready solution. By distilling knowledge from Amazon Nova Pro into the more efficient &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/model-card-amazon-nova-lite.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Nova Lite&lt;/a&gt; model, IBS Software achieved 95.085 percent F1-Score accuracy while reducing operational costs by 14x. This case study details their journey from facing complex open-source implementations to a successful deployment on AWS that now processes cargo email messages in real time.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we share the technical approach using token-based distillation, lessons learned, and deployment architecture. If you face similar bilingual NER challenges, you can benefit from IBS Software’s experience with the &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/model-distillation/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock&lt;/a&gt; knowledge distillation capabilities.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="overview-of-solution"&gt;Overview of solution&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The goal was to build a bilingual NER system capable of extracting 23 entity types from cargo logistics email messages written in English and Japanese. The key entities include:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;AWB (Air Waybill) numbers.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Flight numbers and routes.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Weights (gross, chargeable, dimensional).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Dimensions and volume.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Commodity descriptions.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Shipper and consignee information.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Special handling codes.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Delivery instructions.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The primary risks included maintaining high accuracy across both languages, managing inference costs at scale, and achieving low latency for real-time processing. With the model distillation capabilities of Amazon Bedrock, you can use smaller, faster, and more cost-effective models. These models deliver accuracy for your use case that is comparable to the most advanced models in Amazon Bedrock.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following diagram shows the end-to-end bilingual NER workflow on Amazon Bedrock.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/25/ML-20433-1.png" alt="End-to-end bilingual NER workflow on Amazon Bedrock" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Figure 1: End-to-end bilingual NER workflow on Amazon Bedrock&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="solution"&gt;Solution&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;IBS’s team of nine researchers and engineers spent approximately 4 months developing and deploying this solution. The project timeline included:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Month 1:&lt;/strong&gt; Dataset preparation and annotation of 500 bilingual email messages.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Month 2:&lt;/strong&gt; Challenges with open-source frameworks (PyTorch, TextBrewer).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Month 3:&lt;/strong&gt; Successful distillation using Amazon Bedrock (Nova Pro → Nova Lite).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Month 4:&lt;/strong&gt; Production deployment and optimization.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Key tasks completed:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Annotated 500 cargo email messages (350 English, 150 Japanese) with 23 entity types.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Configured Amazon Bedrock distillation with custom hyperparameters.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Trained student model for 4 epochs over 70 steps.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Achieved loss reduction from 0.05 to 0.008.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Deployed inference endpoint with .eml file processing pipeline.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Validated 95.085 percent F1-Score on test set.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;IBS Software deployed all infrastructure using Amazon Bedrock managed services, which bypassed the need for custom model hosting infrastructure.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="challenges-with-open-source-approaches"&gt;Challenges with open-source approaches&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Initially, the team attempted knowledge distillation using open-source frameworks including PyTorch-based implementations and the TextBrewer library. These approaches failed because of:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Complexity in configuring distillation pipelines for bilingual data.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Lack of managed infrastructure for training and deployment.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Difficulty tuning hyperparameters for token-level distillation.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Incompatibility with our production email processing workflow.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For more details on knowledge distillation fundamentals, see &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/machine-learning/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Machine Learning Best Practices&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="amazon-bedrock-distillation-approach"&gt;Amazon Bedrock distillation approach&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We pivoted to Amazon Bedrock Model Distillation, using Amazon Nova Pro as the teacher model and Nova Lite as the student model. The key advantages included:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Managed training infrastructure with automatic hyperparameter optimization.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Native support for token-level distillation.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Ease of integration with our email processing pipeline.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Built-in monitoring and evaluation metrics.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Training configuration:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;distillation_config = {
    "teacher_model": "amazon.nova-pro-v1:0",
    "student_model": "amazon.nova-lite-v1:0",
    "max_sequence_length": 2048,
    "epochs": 4,
    "training_steps": 70,
    "loss_function": "token_level_kl_divergence"
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The training process reduced loss from 0.05 to 0.008 over 70 steps, indicating strong knowledge transfer from teacher to student.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For Amazon Bedrock distillation documentation, see &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/model-distillation.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Customize a model with distillation in Amazon Bedrock&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="dataset-preparation"&gt;Dataset preparation&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Our dataset consisted of 500 real-world cargo logistics email messages:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;350 English email messages:&lt;/strong&gt; Standard cargo documentation with AWB numbers, flight details, weights, and handling instructions.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;150 Japanese email messages:&lt;/strong&gt; Similar content with Japanese-specific formatting and terminology.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Each email message was manually annotated for 23 entity types by domain experts familiar with cargo logistics terminology. The annotation process took approximately 3 weeks and supplied high-quality training data for both languages.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="model-evaluation"&gt;Model evaluation&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We evaluated both teacher and student models using F1-Score, the harmonic mean of precision and recall:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Results:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Although the base Nova Lite model offered approximately 84 percent overall F1-Score, the teacher model and the customized Nova Lite model achieved an approximately 10 percent uplift in accuracy. The following table shows the F1-Score results.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Model&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Overall F1-Score&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;English F1-Score&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Japanese F1-Score&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Nova Pro (Teacher)&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;97.0%&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;97.8%&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;96.2%&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Nova Lite (Student)&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;95.085%&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;96.535%&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;93.635%&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The distilled Nova Lite model retained 98 percent of the teacher’s performance while providing 14x cost reduction in production inference.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="error-analysis-and-challenges"&gt;Error analysis and challenges&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We observed that the student model showed a 2.565 percent lower F1-Score on Japanese text than on English text. This gap came primarily from complex kanji character combinations in commodity descriptions, ambiguous entity boundaries in Japanese text without spaces, and the smaller volume of Japanese training data (150 compared to 350 email messages). Multi-line delivery instructions with embedded entities also occasionally caused boundary detection errors.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To overcome these challenges, we augmented Japanese training data with synthetic examples. We also applied post-processing rules for known entity patterns (AWB format, flight number regex) and implemented confidence thresholding to flag low-confidence predictions for human review.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="deployment-workflow"&gt;Deployment workflow&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Note:&lt;/strong&gt; The following deployment creates AWS resources that incur charges. Amazon Simple Storage Service (Amazon S3) storage, AWS Lambda invocations, Amazon Bedrock model inference, and Amazon DynamoDB storage all have associated costs. Delete these resources when you no longer need them to avoid ongoing charges.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Our production deployment processes .eml files through the following workflow:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Email ingestion:&lt;/strong&gt; Cargo email messages arrive as .eml files in Amazon S3.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Preprocessing:&lt;/strong&gt; AWS Lambda extracts email body and metadata.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Inference:&lt;/strong&gt; Amazon Bedrock endpoint processes text with distilled Nova Lite model.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Entity extraction:&lt;/strong&gt; Model returns 23 entity types with confidence scores.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Post-processing:&lt;/strong&gt; Validation rules and confidence filtering applied.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Output:&lt;/strong&gt; Structured JSON with extracted entities stored in Amazon DynamoDB.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;import boto3
import json

bedrock_runtime = boto3.client('bedrock-runtime')

def extract_entities(email_text):
    response = bedrock_runtime.invoke_model(
        modelId='&amp;lt;custom model arn&amp;gt;',
        body=json.dumps({
            "inputText": email_text,
            "taskType": "NER",
            "entityTypes": [
                "AWB_NUMBER", "FLIGHT_NUMBER", "WEIGHT_GROSS",
                "WEIGHT_CHARGEABLE", "DIMENSIONS", "COMMODITY",
                "SHIPPER", "CONSIGNEE", "HANDLING_CODE",
                # ... 14 more entity types
            ]
        })
    )

    result = json.loads(response['body'].read())
    return result['entities']&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For Lambda integration patterns, see &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/lambda/latest/dg/services-bedrock.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Lambda with Amazon Bedrock&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The entire pipeline processes email messages in under 2 seconds with 95.085 percent accuracy, meeting our real-time processing requirements.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we showed how IBS Software used Amazon Bedrock managed distillation capabilities to build a cost-effective bilingual NER system for cargo logistics. The system achieves 95.085 percent F1-Score while reducing operational costs by 14x. The distilled Nova Lite model retains 98 percent of the teacher model’s performance, making it ideal for high-volume production workloads.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Our key takeaway was that Amazon Bedrock managed distillation capabilities alleviated the complexity of open-source frameworks. The token-level knowledge distillation preserved accuracy across both English and Japanese, and the 2048-token sequence length accommodated typical cargo email lengths. Production deployment with AWS Lambda and Amazon S3 integration required minimal custom infrastructure.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Next steps:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If you’re facing similar bilingual NER challenges, consider:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Start with Amazon Bedrock on-demand foundation models for rapid prototyping.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Invest in high-quality bilingual training data annotation.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Explore model distillation with the training dataset. One limitation of model distillation is that the teacher model and the student model must be within the same model family.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For more information about the topics discussed in this post, see the following resources:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/a-guide-to-amazon-bedrock-model-distillation-preview/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;A guide to Amazon Bedrock Model Distillation&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/accelerating-custom-entity-recognition-with-claude-tool-use-in-amazon-bedrock/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Accelerating custom entity recognition with Claude tool use in Amazon Bedrock&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/build-an-ai-powered-document-processing-platform-with-open-source-ner-model-and-llm-on-amazon-sagemaker/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Build an AI-powered document processing platform with open-source NER model and LLM on Amazon SageMaker AI&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If you’re working on bilingual NER or knowledge distillation for your own use case, we’d love to hear about your experience. Share your questions or feedback in the comments.&lt;/p&gt;

		<item>
		<title>Fine-tune Amazon Nova models for accurate email data extraction</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/fine-tune-amazon-nova-models-for-accurate-email-data-extraction/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Le Vy]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 30 Jun 2026 16:26:48 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Amazon Bedrock]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon SageMaker AI]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Simple Storage Service (S3)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[AWS Identity and Access Management (IAM)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Customer Solutions]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">e810a0ce533913bea7dc4cf63244fe692ea6e3d0</guid>

					<description>In this post, you'll learn how fine-tuning Amazon Nova models using Amazon SageMaker AI addresses these specific issues by teaching the models to recognize your exact data patterns, distinguish between similar fields, and process information more efficiently—achieving up to 94.77% extraction accuracy while reducing costs 50%.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;The authors would also like to thank Karan Bhandarkar, Sue Cha, Yash Shah&lt;/em&gt;&lt;em&gt; and &lt;/em&gt;&lt;em&gt;Nieves Garcia&lt;/em&gt;&lt;em&gt; for their contributions in making this initiative possible.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If you process millions of email messages daily, fine-tuning Amazon Nova models can help you automate accurate data extraction while reducing costs and hallucinations. &lt;a href="https://www.parcelperform.com/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Parcel Perform&lt;/a&gt;, a leading AI Delivery Experience Platform for ecommerce businesses worldwide, faced this exact challenge when extracting structured information from diverse email formats, ranging from simple notifications to complex HTML documents with extensive JavaScript elements.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Common challenges include model hallucinations, confusion between similar data types (such as order numbers and tracking numbers), and prohibitively high token costs when processing HTML-formatted email.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, you’ll learn how fine-tuning Amazon Nova models using Amazon SageMaker AI addresses these specific issues by teaching the models to recognize your exact data patterns, distinguish between similar fields, and process information more efficiently—achieving up to 94.77% extraction accuracy while reducing costs 50%.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="the-collaboration"&gt;The collaboration&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Parcel Perform worked with the AWS Generative AI Innovation Center (GenAIIC), which provides business and technical consultancy throughout the customer journey. Working backward from Parcel Perform’s problem statement, the team scoped a project to optimize Nova models through various customization techniques and parameter optimization.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This collaboration allowed concurrent improvement of multiple metrics: accuracy, latency, and cost. Le Vy, AI Team Lead at Parcel Perform, reported that the fine-tuned Nova Micro models achieved up to 94.77% extraction accuracy on the testing dataset, an improvement of up to 16.6 percentage points over the baseline. The fine-tuned Nova Micro reduced inference latency by more than 30 percent and halved costs compared with Parcel Perform’s previous model, while matching or exceeding the fine-tuned Nova Lite model at lower cost. With these results, Parcel Perform moved the solution into production to improve its e-commerce logistics operations.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="solution-overview"&gt;Solution overview&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can use Amazon SageMaker AI custom model fine-tuning to adapt Amazon Nova Lite and Amazon Nova Micro models for specialized entity extraction from ecommerce email. This solution uses supervised fine-tuning (SFT) with Parameter-Efficient Fine-Tuning (PEFT) through Low-Rank Adaptation (LoRA). With PEFT, you can customize models effectively with limited training data while maintaining computational efficiency.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can also use PEFT to deploy the model into Amazon Bedrock and invoke it with on-demand inference priced per token. Amazon Bedrock offers flexible deployment options: with PEFT, you can deploy using &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/deploy-custom-model-on-demand.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;on-demand inference&lt;/a&gt;, while full-rank SFT supports deployment through either &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/prov-throughput.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Provisioned Throughput on Amazon Bedrock&lt;/a&gt; or a &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/deploy-model.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;SageMaker AI endpoint&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The custom model fine-tuning uses &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/nova/latest/nova2-userguide/nova-model-recipes.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Nova recipes&lt;/a&gt;, which are YAML configuration files that provide details to Amazon SageMaker AI on how to run your model customization job, including the base model name, training hyperparameters, optimization settings, and additional options. The following diagram illustrates the solution architecture.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/10/ML-20559-1.png" alt="drawing" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Here’s how the workflow works:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Prepare training data in the Amazon Bedrock conversation format with email content as input and extracted entities as output.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Upload training data to Amazon Simple Storage Service (Amazon S3).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Create a fine-tuning job in SageMaker AI using Low-Rank Adaptation (LoRA) configuration.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Deploy the fine-tuned model using Amazon Bedrock with on-demand inference.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Run inference to extract entities from new email.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="prerequisites"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You will need the following to fine-tune and deploy Nova models:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;AWS account with appropriate permissions&lt;/strong&gt;: Required to access AWS services and create resources.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Access to Amazon Bedrock and Amazon Nova models&lt;/strong&gt;: Must be available in your chosen AWS Region.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;IAM service role&lt;/strong&gt;: An AWS Identity and Access Management (IAM) role with permissions for Amazon Bedrock model customization.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;S3 bucket&lt;/strong&gt;: For storing training data and output artifacts.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Training data in JSONL format&lt;/strong&gt;: Follow the data format and preparation guidance.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Sufficient Service Quotas&lt;/strong&gt;: Establish adequate quota for your chosen instance type and size in SageMaker AI Training.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For instructions on creating the service role, see &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/model-customization-iam-role.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Create a service role for model customization&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="prepare-the-training-data"&gt;Prepare the training data&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Your training data must follow the Amazon Bedrock conversation schema format. Each sample contains the email content as user input and the extracted entities as the assistant response.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Here’s a simplified example of the required format:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{
  "schemaVersion": "bedrock-conversation-2024",
  "system": [
    {
      "text": "You are an expert at extracting structured data from e-commerce order emails. Extract all relevant fields accurately without fabricating any information."
    }
  ],
  "messages": [
    {
      "role": "user",
      "content": [
        {
          "text": "&amp;lt;email_content&amp;gt;Your order #12345 has shipped! Track your package: TRK789456123&amp;lt;/email_content&amp;gt;"
        }
      ]
    },
    {
      "role": "assistant",
      "content": [
        {
          "text": "{\"order_number\": \"12345\", \"tracking_number\": \"TRK789456123\", \"status\": \"shipped\"}"
        }
      ]
    }
  ]
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In our experiment, we prepared two training datasets: a smaller set with 1,300 samples and a larger set with 4,900 samples. This allowed us to evaluate how training data size impacts model performance.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can use Amazon SageMaker Studio as your development environment to run Jupyter Notebook for the data preparation step.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="upload-training-data-to-an-s3-bucket"&gt;Upload training data to an S3 bucket&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;SageMaker AI Training runs jobs in separate environments from your code and data development environment. With SageMaker AI Training, you can efficiently use one or multiple powerful GPU-powered instances that automatically stop when training finishes. Because the instances are separate from your data preparation environment, you will need to upload the data to a centralized location: Amazon S3.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following code illustrates uploading data from SageMaker Studio to an S3 bucket:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;import boto3

s3 = boto3.client('s3')
s3.upload_file('train.jsonl', 'your-training-bucket', 'train.jsonl')&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="fine-tune-the-amazon-nova-model"&gt;Fine-tune the Amazon Nova model&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;With your training data prepared, you can create a fine-tuning job using the Amazon SageMaker AI API through the SageMaker SDK. Follow the examples in the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/amazon-nova-samples/tree/main/customization" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Nova Customization Hub&lt;/a&gt;. This provisions a training job with Amazon SageMaker AI Training that runs on a container on one or multiple separate instances with the type and size you choose.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following table summarizes the key fine-tuning parameters in our experiment:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Parameters&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Description&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;model_type&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;“amazon.nova-lite-v1:0:300k” or “amazon.nova-micro-v1:0:128k”&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;model_name_or_path&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;“nova-lite/prod” or “nova-micro/prod”&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;replicas&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;1 for g5/g6 4 for p5&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;max_length&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;8192 for g5/g6 32768 for p5&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;global_batch_size&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;64&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;max_epochs&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;2&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;peft_scheme&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;lora&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;loraplus_lr_ratio&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;8.0&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;alpha&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;32&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We selected Nova Lite and Nova Micro because of their lower cost and competitive performance compared with Amazon Nova Pro and other models.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="deploy-and-run-inference"&gt;Deploy and run inference&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When your fine-tuning job completes successfully, you can create a custom model in Amazon Bedrock to import the PEFT-tuned Nova model trained by Amazon SageMaker AI. Follow &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/create-custom-model-sdks.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Create a custom model&lt;/a&gt; for detailed steps. Then you can run inference using the custom model as shown in the following example:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;import boto3
import json

bedrock_runtime = boto3.client(service_name="bedrock-runtime")

def extract_entities(email_content, provisioned_model_arn):
    body = {
        "messages": [{
            "role": "user",
            "content": f"Extract all relevant data fields from this email:\n\n{email_content}"
        }],
        "max_tokens": 2048,
        "temperature": 0.1
    }

    response = bedrock_runtime.invoke_model(
        body=json.dumps(body),
        modelId=provisioned_model_arn,
        accept="application/json",
        contentType="application/json"
    )

    response_body = json.loads(response.get('body').read())
    return response_body['output']['message']['content'][0]['text']

# Example usage
email = """
Dear Customer,
Your order has been shipped!
Carrier: FedEx
Estimated Delivery: January 15, 2025
"""

result = extract_entities(email, "arn:aws:bedrock:us-east-1:${account-id}:provisioned-model/your-model-id")&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The function returns extracted fields similar to the following:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;{
  Carrier: FedEx,
  Date: January 15, 2025
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You also have the option of iteratively training the model with a different method. For example, you can use the checkpoint from SFT-PEFT tuning as a base for downstream Direct Preference Optimization (DPO) training. See the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/nova-iterative-training.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;documentation on iterative training&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="evaluation-results"&gt;Evaluation results&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Fine-tuned models improved accuracy by 5.6–16.6 percentage points over baselines, with Nova Micro achieving the highest overall accuracy of 94.77 percent despite being the smaller model. Fine-tuning also reduced inference latency by approximately 32 percent and cut costs by approximately 50 percent through PEFT tuning on lighter models compared to existing models with pay-per-use pricing. These combined gains in accuracy, speed, and cost enabled Parcel Perform to deploy the tuned Amazon Nova model into production.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="accuracy-comparison"&gt;Accuracy comparison&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Parcel Perform evaluated the fine-tuned models against baseline models using Parcel Perform’s weighted accuracy metric, which combines extraction accuracy across the data fields. We tested on two representative datasets with 100 samples and 200 samples, respectively.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Key findings:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Fine-tuning delivered accuracy gains across all models, with improvements ranging from 5.6 to 16.6 percentage points over their baseline counterparts. Nova Micro showed the largest jump, climbing from 76.63 percent to 93.27 percent after fine-tuning. Scaling the training data from 1,300 to 4,900 samples further boosted performance by up to 3.3 percent, showing that modest increases in training volume continue to yield meaningful returns. Despite being the smaller model, Nova Micro achieved the highest overall accuracy of 94.77 percent on the 200-sample test set. A well-tuned compact model can outperform larger alternatives on domain-specific tasks.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="latency-improvements"&gt;Latency improvements&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Fine-tuning also reduced inference latency significantly: 31 percent for Nova Lite and 32 percent for Nova Micro because of Nova Micro’s smaller model size and fewer parameters compared with Parcel Perform’s previous model. This represents about 7.7 seconds per inference, making it faster to process large volumes of email daily.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="impact"&gt;Impact&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Processing time decreased significantly compared with Parcel Perform’s previous model. Costs reduced by approximately 50 percent because PEFT lets lighter models perform better at specific tasks while supporting deployment with pay-per-use pricing. The improved accuracy, paired with reduced cost and latency, lets Parcel Perform use the fine-tuned Amazon Nova model in production.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we demonstrated how you can fine-tune Amazon Nova models using Amazon SageMaker AI for accurate entity extraction from e-commerce email. Our collaboration with Parcel Perform showed that Parameter-Efficient Fine-Tuning using LoRA can achieve significant accuracy improvements while reducing inference latency by more than 30 percent.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Key takeaways from this engagement:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Fine-tuning proved effective at reducing hallucinations. The models correctly distinguished between order numbers and tracking numbers without fabricating data that doesn’t exist in the source material. You don’t need massive datasets to see real results: meaningful accuracy gains were achieved with as few as 1,300 training samples across 25 entities, making fine-tuning a practical option even for teams with limited labeled data. In a counterintuitive finding, the smaller Nova Micro model outperformed the larger Nova Lite after fine-tuning, showing that task-specific optimization can more than compensate for differences in base model size. This has cost implications: pairing a smaller, faster model like Nova Micro with reduced inference latency creates a cost-effective solution for processing large volumes of email at scale. The ability to deploy PEFT-tuned models directly into Amazon Bedrock with on-demand, token-based pricing means you can run a fully customized model on a pay-per-use basis, removing the need to provision and maintain dedicated LLM-hosting infrastructure.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="get-started"&gt;Get started&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To implement this solution, start by preparing your training data in the preceding format. Begin with a dataset of at least 1,300 samples as shown in this post for meaningful results. To get optimal results, make sure your training data represents the variety of email formats that you will encounter in production.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If you face similar entity extraction challenges, you can use SageMaker AI model customization to build accurate, production-ready solutions. This approach shows how you can adapt fast and cost-effective models like Nova Micro and Nova Lite for production use cases that deliver accuracy, speed, and cost-effectiveness.&lt;/p&gt;

&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/custom-models.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Customize models in Amazon Bedrock&lt;/a&gt;: Learn more about Amazon Bedrock model customization.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/nova/forge" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Nova Forge product details page&lt;/a&gt;: Discover custom model development using Nova.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://app.qbs-cell001.word.us-east-1.prod.plato.ai.aws.dev/link" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Generative AI Innovation Center&lt;/a&gt;: Work with AWS GenAIIC on similar use cases.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt;

		<pubDate>Mon, 29 Jun 2026 18:15:14 +0000</pubDate>
